The Permissible for Allah: Doing or Leaving Possible Things
Sanusi defines the permissible for Allah as doing or leaving any possible thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational meaning of the permissible (jā'iz) attribute for Allah and how it affirms His absolute freedom of action.

Core Idea
Nothing constraints Allah except impossibility; He freely does or leaves any possible thing.

After covering the necessary and impossible, Sanusi now defines the permissible: what Allah may freely do or leave. This one concise statement completes the three rational categories and establishes that nothing compels or restricts Allah's actions beyond the impossibilities already stated. Let's examine this crucial definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: 'As for the permissible... it is doing every possible thing or leaving it.'
The word jā'iz here is a technical term in kalām.
2
Understand the definition
Sanusi defines the permissible as two options: either doing a possible thing or not doing it. This means that for any possible action, Allah can either will it or not will it without external constraint.
This directly contrasts with wajib (must exist) and mustaḥīl (cannot exist).
3
Contrast with necessary and impossible
Recall: necessary is what must be, impossible is what can't be. The permissible is what can be or not be. For Allah, the permissible includes all actions on possible objects — He is not forced to create or not create.
4
Reflect on divine sovereignty
Consider why this category is essential: it affirms that Allah's actions are not necessitated by any external cause. His freedom is absolute within the realm of possibility.
This guards against views that God must create or must act in a certain way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا الجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعالى فَفِعلُ كلّ مُمكنٍ أو تركه.
And as for the permissible (jāʾiz) in His right, exalted, it is doing every possible thing or leaving it.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the permissible (jāʾiz) attribute for Allah. After enumerating the necessary and impossible attributes, Sanusi states that the permissible is that Allah may do or leave any possible action. This means that nothing is obligatory or impossible for Him other than what has been specified. His actions are not constrained by any external necessity; He has absolute freedom to create or not create, to bring about any possible state of affairs. This category underscores divine sovereignty.

Key Terms
جائز
فعل
ممكن
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'permissible' implies God is indifferent about whether He acts or not.
Correction: The permissible does not indicate indifference; it indicates that no external necessity forces Allah to act or refrain. He acts with perfect wisdom and will, freely choosing among possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the permissible (jāʾiz) for Allah?
A) Necessary attributes only
B) Doing every possible thing or leaving it
C) Creating only good things
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
The permissible category affirms that Allah freely chooses to do or leave any possible action, unconstrained by external necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often question why Allah did not give you something you desperately wanted. This lesson teaches that it is permissible for Allah to leave any possible thing. Are you truly at peace with the fact that Allah's leaving is as much a wise choice as His giving? Or is there a part of you that still believes He 'owes' you an explanation? Commit today to recite 'faʿala llāhu mā yashāʾ' (Allah does what He wills) when dissatisfaction arises.
?
This lesson says Allah's actions are not constrained by external necessity. Yet you sometimes act as if Allah has certain obligations to fulfill your expectations. Where in your life do you hold God to a standard of what you think is 'necessary' for Him to do? Write down one area and surrender it to Him right now.
?
If you truly believe that Allah is free to do or leave every possible thing, then your provision, your health, your future — all are in His hands. How does this truth confront your anxiety? What is one step you can take today to live out this trust?

Proof of God's Existence via Contingency of the World
Sanusi presents a three-step proof from the contingency of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves God's existence by demonstrating that the world is contingent and therefore requires a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's need for a cause is proven by its inseparable connection to changing accidents.

This lesson presents Sanusi's foundational argument for God's existence. The proof proceeds in clear logical steps: first, the world must be contingent because otherwise, it would need to prefer its own existence without reason. Then, the world is shown to be contingent because it is always accompanied by changing accidents. Finally, the accidents themselves are shown to be contingent because we observe them change. By the end, the necessity of a Creator becomes rationally inescapable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three parts of the proof
The passage has three distinct parts: the overall proof (if no Creator, impossible preference), the proof of the world's contingency (via accidents), and the proof of accidents' contingency (via observation). Find each part.
3
Understand the core logical move
The key insight is that if the world had no cause, then one of two equally possible states (existence vs. non-existence) would be preferred without any reason, which is impossible. This forces the existence of a Creator.
This argument is known as the 'preference without a cause' argument.
4
Trace the chain of contingency
Work backwards: accidents change → they are contingent. The world is always accompanied by accidents → the world is contingent. A contingent world needs a cause → that cause is God.
This chain shows that the proof is airtight if each link is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما برهان وجوده تعالى: فحدوث العالم؛ لأنه لو لم يكن له محدث، بل حدث بنفسه لزم أن يكون أحد الأمرين المتساويين مساوياً لصاحبه راجحاً عليه بلا سبب، وهو محال. ودليل حدوث العالم: ملازمته للأعراض الحادثة من حركة وسكون وغيرهما، وملازم الحادث حادث. ودليل حدوث الأعراض: مشاهدة تغيرها من عدم إلى وجود، ومن وجود إلى عدم.
As for the proof of His existence, exalted: it is the contingency of the world, because if it had no originator, but rather came into existence by itself, it would follow that one of two equal things would be preferable to its counterpart without a cause, and that is impossible. And the proof of the contingency of the world is its inseparable association with contingent accidents such as motion and rest and others, and the concomitant of the contingent is contingent. And the proof of the contingency of accidents is the observation of their change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage provides the proof (burhān) for God's existence. The argument is based on the contingency (ḥudūth) of the world. Sanusi first states the core premise: the world is contingent. He then gives the reason: if the world had no creator but came to be by itself, then given two equally possible states (e.g., existence and non-existence at a particular time), one would have to be preferred over the other without any cause—which is rationally impossible. Therefore, a creator (Muḥdith) must exist. Next, he proves the world's contingency. The world is defined as everything other than God. Its contingency is shown by its inseparable connection with accidents (aʿrāḍ) that are themselves contingent—like motion and rest. Whatever is always accompanied by the contingent is itself contingent. Finally, he proves that accidents are contingent by observing that they change from non-existence to existence and vice versa, which is the definition of contingency. Thus the world is contingent and requires a Creator.

Key Terms
برهان
حدوث
عالم
أعراض
ملازمة
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world means only the planet Earth, and contingency means having a beginning in time.
Correction: In this context, 'world' refers to everything other than God, and 'contingency' (ḥudūth) means essential dependence on a cause, not necessarily a temporal beginning. The proof does not rely on the world's age but on its need for a cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say the world must be contingent?
A) Because it is composed of atoms
B) Because it is inseparable from contingent accidents
C) Because it was created by God
D) Because it has a beginning

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's proof establishes God's existence by showing that the world's dependence on changing accidents proves its contingency, which in turn necessitates a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the world is self-sufficient, or do you constantly recognize that everything you see and experience is utterly dependent on God in every moment? What would change in your daily habits if you truly internalized this dependency?
?
When you observe the constant change in your own body, your emotions, and your circumstances, do you see these as evidence of contingency? How can you use those moments to remind yourself of the Creator's existence?
?
If you honestly believe that without God's constant origination nothing could exist, why do you still sometimes act as if your accomplishments or possessions are due to your own power? How can you train yourself to attribute all existence to Him?

Proof of God's Necessary Pre-Eternity
Sanusi proves God's pre-eternity via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God must be pre-eternal, and why alternative scenarios lead to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
If God had a beginning, He would need a creator—leading to an impossible circle or infinite chain.

Sanusi now turns to proving that God is not only existent but also pre-eternal (qadīm). The argument is a simple but powerful reductio: if God were not eternal, He would be a contingent being, requiring an originator, which would create an infinite regress or vicious circle. This brief passage lays the logical foundation for the attribute of pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the concise structure of the reductio argument.
The argument is only two lines but contains a complete logical chain.
2
Identify the conditional chain
Trace the 'if-then' reasoning: if not pre-eternal → contingent → needs an originator → leads to vicious circle or infinite regress.
Each step follows necessarily from the previous one.
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Understand why both a vicious circle (dawr) and an infinite regress (tasalsul) are rationally impossible. This forces the conclusion that God must be pre-eternal.
In kalam, these two are the only alternatives to a First Cause, and both are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب القدم له تعالى: فلأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، فيفتقر إلى محدث، فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل.
As for the proof of the necessity of pre-eternity (qidam) for Him, exalted: it is because if He were not pre-eternal, He would be contingent, and thus would need an originator, and then there would follow a vicious circle or an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
Sanusi gives a succinct proof that God must be pre-eternal (qadīm). The argument is a reductio: if God were not pre-eternal, He would be contingent (ḥādith), because everything that is not eternal is originated. But if He were originated, He would need an originator (muḥdith). That originator would either be God Himself (leading to a vicious circle: God originates Himself) or something else, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of creators. Both are impossible. Therefore, God must be pre-eternal, without beginning.

Key Terms
قدم
حدوث
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity means existing outside of time in a philosophical sense.
Correction: In this context, pre-eternity (qidam) simply means without beginning. It does not necessarily imply timelessness in the sense of being outside time.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would follow if God were not pre-eternal?
A) He would be composed
B) He would need an originator, leading to circularity or regress
C) He would be multiple
D) He would be powerless

Key Takeaway
God must be pre-eternal because the alternative leads to an impossible vicious circle or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God alone is pre-eternal and uncaused, why do you still attach ultimate value to created things that will perish?
?
When you worry about a problem, do you act as if that problem is the 'first cause' of your anxiety, rather than turning to the only One who has no beginning and no need?
?
This argument shows that only God can be the ultimate originator. Is there any area of your life where you are trying to be your own originator, relying on yourself rather than on the Creator?

Proof of God's Oneness
Sanusi proves God's oneness via a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's oneness is necessary for the existence of the world, based on Sanusi's concise proof.

Core Idea
If God were not one, the world would not exist.

Sanusi presents a powerful logical argument for God's oneness. The reasoning is simple but profound: if there were more than one God, they would conflict, leading to the non-existence of the world. This passage gives the core of that proof, which is central to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Note that it is a single conditional argument.
The argument is very condensed.
2
Identify the logical components
Break down the statement: the premise (if not one) and the conclusion (nothing would exist). See how they are linked.
The phrase 'لزم أن لا يوجد' shows the necessary consequence.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why does this prove oneness? Consider the assumption behind the argument about mutual hindrance.
This is known as burhān al-tamānuʿ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب الوحدانية له تعالى: فلأنه لو لم يكن واحداً لزم أن لا يوجد شيء من العالم للزوم عجزه حينئذٍ.
As for the proof of the necessity of oneness (waḥdāniyya) for Him, exalted: it is because if He were not one, it would follow that nothing of the world would exist, due to the necessity of His inability at that time.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
Sanusi provides a concise proof for God's oneness (waḥdāniyya). The argument is: if there were more than one god, and they disagreed on creating something, then if both wanted opposite outcomes, one would have to prevail. If both are equally powerful, neither could impose their will, resulting in stalemate and the non-existence of the world. Alternatively, if one could overcome the other, the weaker would not be God. Thus, only one God can exist, for otherwise the world would not exist as we see it. This is a classic kalam argument known as the 'proof of mutual hindrance' (burhān al-tamānuʿ).

Key Terms
وحدانية
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of oneness claims multiple beings cannot exist at all.
Correction: The proof shows that if multiple independent gods existed, they would conflict, not that multiple beings cannot exist.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say would happen if there were more than one god?
A) The world would be perfect
B) Nothing of the world would exist
C) The gods would cooperate
D) The gods would be equal

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is necessary because multiple gods would lead to the non-existence of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's existence depends on God being one, how does that change the way you view any form of associating partners with Him? What would it mean for the universe if you were to compromise on tawheed?
?
When you observe the order of the world around you, do you see it as a constant testimony to God's oneness? What would it take for you to live with that awareness every moment?
?
This proof shows that shirk is not only a sin but a logical impossibility for the world to exist. If you truly believed that, would you still struggle with hidden forms of shirk in your heart? What are those forms, and what steps will you take to root them out?

Proof for God's Necessary Eternity
Sanusi gives a proof that God must be eternal, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that God must be eternal, and why denying eternity leads to an absurdity.

Core Idea
If God were not eternal, an impossible chain of creators would follow.

Sanusi now presents the key proof that God's eternity (qidam) is necessary. This is a foundational argument in kalam: it uses the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress to establish that the Creator must be eternal. The passage is concise, so we will unpack its logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once to get its rhythm. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if not eternal then temporal'.
2
Identify the contradiction
The proof assumes the opposite (God not eternal) and shows it leads to an impossibility. Identify that impossibility: circularity or infinite regress.
Both circularity and infinite regress are considered impossible in kalam.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: if not eternal → temporal → needs a creator → that creator is either God (circularity) or another (infinite regress). Both are absurd, so the original assumption is false.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion: God must be eternal. Ask yourself why this proof is important for establishing God's necessary existence.
This proof is often used as the first step in proving God's necessary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجوبِ القِدَمِ لَهُ تعالى: فَلأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيماً لكانَ حادِثاً، فيفتقِرُ إلى مُحِدْثٍ، فيلزمُ الدَّوْرُ أو التّسلسل.
As for the proof of the necessity of eternity for Him, Most High: because if He were not eternal, He would be temporal, and thus would need a creator, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's necessary attribute of eternity (qidam). It is a proof by contradiction: if God were not eternal, He would be temporal (hadith). A temporal being requires a creator (muhdith). That creator would either be God Himself, leading to circularity, or another creator, leading to an infinite regress—both impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal. This is a foundational argument in kalam for establishing God's necessary existence.

Key Terms
قِدَم
حادث
مُحِدْث
دَوْر
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove (that God is eternal).
Correction: The proof does not assume eternity; it assumes the opposite and shows it leads to absurdity, thus proving eternity indirectly. It is a valid proof by contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's proof, what would follow if God were not eternal?
A) He would be self-sufficient.
B) He would need a creator, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
C) He would be composed of parts.
D) He would be limited in power.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress or circularity in the chain of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the chain of causes cannot regress infinitely, then your very existence depends on a First Cause that is uncaused. Do you live with the awareness that every breath you take is sustained directly by that eternal Being, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that God must be eternal, otherwise absurdity follows. Does your heart truly acknowledge God as the only uncreated reality? Where in your life do you still look to created things for ultimate security, as if they could be a first cause for your well-being?
?
This argument is a rational certainty, as clear as 'a circle cannot be a square'. Can you say with the same conviction 'I know God exists'? If not, what is holding you back from embracing this logical necessity as personal faith?

Proof for God's Necessary Perpetuity
Sanusi proves that God must be perpetual, as possibility of non-existence would negate eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God must be perpetual, based on the impossibility of non-existence contradicting His necessary eternity.

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He would not be eternal—so He must be perpetual.

Having established God's necessary existence and eternity, Sanusi now proves that this existence must continue without end. The argument is concise but decisive: the possibility of non-existence would contradict His eternity. Let us carefully unpack this proof to see how it secures the attribute of baqa' (perpetuity).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the proof sink in before examining its parts.
Notice that the passage begins by stating what it will prove: the necessity of perpetuity.
2
Identify the key logical terms
Locate the terms for perpetuity (بقاء), non-existence (عدَم), possible (جائز), necessary (واجب), and eternity (قِدَم). Understanding these is essential to follow the argument.
These terms form the backbone of the proof.
3
Trace the conditional argument
Follow Sanusi's reasoning: if non-existence were possible, then God's existence becomes contingent (ja'iz) rather than necessary (wajib). Contingent existence is temporal. This contradicts God's proven eternity.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the previous proof
Recall that God's existence was shown to be necessary and eternal (qidam). This proof builds on that foundation: if God is eternal, He cannot be subject to non-existence.
Perpetuity is a necessary consequence of eternity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that baqa' is not merely long duration but necessary endless existence. Consider why this attribute is essential to the Islamic conception of God.
This guards against thinking God could ever cease to be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ البَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعالى : فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ العَدَمُ لانتفى عَنهُ القِدَمُ، لِكَوْنِ وُجودِهِ حينئذٍ يَصيرُ جَائِزاً لا وَاجِباً، والجَائِزُ لا يَكونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلا حَادِثاً، كيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَريباً وُجوبُ قِدَمهِ تَعالى وبقائه.
As for the proof of the necessity of perpetuity for Him, Most High: because if non-existence could affect Him, eternity would be negated from Him, since His existence would then become possible (ja'iz) not necessary (wajib), and the existence of the possible is only temporal, especially since the necessity of His eternity and perpetuity has been established recently.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for God's necessary attribute of perpetuity (baqa'). The argument: If non-existence were possible for God, then His existence would be contingent (ja'iz) rather than necessary (wajib). But a contingent existence is temporal by nature. Since we have already proven that God is eternal (qidam), and that eternity implies necessary existence, it follows that He cannot be subject to non-existence. Thus, He must be perpetual.

Key Terms
بقاء
عدَم
جائز
واجب
قِدَم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perpetuity (baqa') means simply a very long duration, but God could eventually cease to exist.
Correction: In kalam, baqa' is a necessary attribute of God meaning endless existence that follows necessarily from His eternity; it cannot be otherwise.

Checkpoint
Why would the possibility of non-existence negate God's eternity according to this proof?
A) Because non-existence is an attribute of perfection.
B) Because possibility implies contingency, and contingent existence is temporal.
C) Because eternity and non-existence are the same.
D) Because God's power would be limited.

Key Takeaway
God's perpetuity is a necessary attribute derived from His necessary existence and eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God is perpetual and cannot cease to exist, how would that change the way you face your own mortality?
?
The passage argues that God's existence is necessary and perpetual. Does your life reflect a trust in a God who is always there, or do you act as if He might disappear?
?
Think of a moment when you felt abandoned. Does the truth that God's existence is necessary and eternal reassure you that He never leaves? How can you remind yourself of this truth daily?

Proof for God's Unity (Wahdaniyya)
Sanusi proves God's unity by showing that multiplicity leads to incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's concise proof for God's unity and why multiplicity leads to incapacity.

Core Idea
If God were not one, nothing would exist.

Sanusi presents a compact yet powerful proof for God's unity. The argument is based on the observed existence of the world: if there were multiple gods, they would each be incapable of acting, resulting in nothing existing. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Carefully read the original Arabic and its translation. Notice the logical structure: if not one, then nothing.
2
Identify the premises
The proof relies on a conditional: if God were not one, then incapacity follows. Then from incapacity, non-existence follows. Identify these two steps.
3
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why this is a standard kalam argument. Why does multiplicity imply incapacity? How does this prove tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بُرْهانُ وُجُوبِ الوَحْدانيةِ لهُ تعالى: فَلأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يكنْ واحِداً لزِمَ أنْ لا يُوجَدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ العالم لِلزُوم عجزِهِ حينئذٍ.
As for the proof of the necessity of unity for Him, Most High: because if He were not one, it would necessitate that nothing of the world would exist, due to the impossibility of His being incapable then.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage proves God's unity (wahdaniyya). The proof: If God were not one, meaning there were more than one god, then nothing in the world would exist. The reason is that if there were two gods, each would be incapable of acting independently, leading to a deadlock. The existence of the world, therefore, proves that God is one.

Key Terms
وحدانية
واحد
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would follow if God were not one?
A) The world would be created by multiple gods.
B) Nothing in the world would exist.
C) There would be no need for worship.
D) God would have partners.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves God's unity by showing that any multiplicity would lead to incapacity, and the existence of the world necessitates that God is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If multiple gods were possible, you would not exist. How does this truth affect the way you view your very existence as a direct gift from the One who alone makes it possible?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but where do you actually place your trust—in people, wealth, or yourself? How does this lesson expose the hidden shirk in your heart?
?
The proof shows that incapacity is impossible for God. Yet you often act as if Allah might not come through for you. What would it mean to truly live as if His unity demands that He alone is the source of all power?

The Shahada: Encompassing All Creeds
Sanusi defines divinity and encapsulates it in the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shahada captures the essence of all prior creeds through the concepts of Allah's self-sufficiency and the dependency of all else.

Core Idea
The shahada defines the very meaning of divinity as self-sufficiency and universal dependency.

Before diving into specific attributes, Sanusi teaches us that the entire creed is summed up in the testimony of faith. He defines what it means for Allah to be divine—self-sufficient and the one upon whom all depend. This sets the foundation for understanding why certain attributes are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning of the shahada and uluhiyya.
Notice that the passage is concise and definitional.
2
Understand the definition of uluhiyya
Sanusi explains that 'uluhiyya' consists of two aspects: Allah's self-sufficiency (istighna') and the dependency of all else (iftiqar). Ensure you can articulate this in your own words.
These two aspects are the pillars from which all attributes follow.
3
Connect to the shahada
The shahada 'la ilaha illallah' negates any self-sufficient besides Allah and affirms that all others depend on Him. See how this simple statement carries profound meaning.
This shows the comprehensiveness of the testimony of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجْمَعُ مَعَانِي هذهِ العَقَائدِ كُلِّهَا قَوْلُ: لا إله إلا الله مُحمَّدٌ رَسُولُ الله؛ إذْ مَعْنَى الألوهِيَّةِ: استغناء الإلَهِ عِن كُلّ ما سواه، وافتقارُ كلّ مَا عَدَاهُ إلَيْهِ. فَمَعْنى لا إله إلا الله لا مُسْتَغْنياً عنْ مَا سِواه، ومُفْتَقِراً إليهِ كُلُّ مَا عَدَاهُ إِلا الله تعالى. أمَّا استغناؤُهُ جلّ وعَلاَ عَنْ كلّ ما سواه فهوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تعالى الوُجودَ والقدمَ والبَقاء والمُخَالَفَةَ للحَوَادِثِ والقِيامَ بِالنَّفس والتَنزُّه عن النقائص. ويدخل في ذلك: وُجوبُ الشمع لَهُ تعالى والبَصرِ وَالكلام؛ إذ لو لَمْ تَجِبْ لَهُ تَعالى هذِهِ الصَّفاتُ لكان مُحتاجاً إلى المُحْدِثِ، أو المحلّ، أو مَنْ يَدفعُ عَنهُ النَّقائِصَ. وَيؤخذُ مِنْهُ: تنزُّهُهُ تَعالى عَنِ الأعْراضِ في أفعاله وأحكامه، وإلا لزِمَ افتقارُهُ إِلى مَا يُحصِل غَرَضَهُ، كيفَ وهُوَ جلّ وعَلا الغَنِيُّ عَنْ كُلَّ مَا سواه؟ ويُؤخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً أَنَّهُ لا يجِبُ عَلَيْهِ تَعالى فِعْلُ شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُمْكِناتِ وَلا تركه؛ إذ لَوْ وَجَبَ عَلَيْهِ تعالى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا عَقْلاً كالنَّوابِ مَثَلاً لكانَ جَلْ وعزّ مُفتقراً إلى ذلك الشَّيء لِيَتَكَمَّل بِهِ؛ إذْ لا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ جَلّ وَعَزّ إِلا ما هُوَ كمال له، كيف وهُوَ الغَني جلّ وَعَلا عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سَواهُ؟ وأمَّا افتقارُ كُلِّ مَا سِواهُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الحَياةَ وَعمُومَ القُدْرَةِ والإِرَادَة والعِلمِ؛ إذْ لَوِ انتفى شَيْءٌ مِنها لَما أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجَدَ
And the meanings of all these creeds are encompassed by the statement: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' For the meaning of divinity (uluhiyya) is the self-sufficiency of the god from everything other than Him, and the dependency of everything other than Him upon Him. So the meaning of 'la ilaha illallah' is: there is no one self-sufficient from what is other than Him, and every other than Him is dependent upon Him, except Allah. As for His self-sufficiency, may He be glorified and exalted, from everything other than Him, it necessitates for Him, exalted, existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to originated things, self-subsistence, and transcendence from deficiencies. And included in that is the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech for Him; for if these attributes were not necessary for Him, He would be in need of the originator, or of a locus, or of one who repels deficiencies from Him. And from it is taken: His transcendence, exalted, from accidents in His actions and judgments; otherwise, His dependence would be necessary upon what achieves His purpose, how could that be when He is the Self-Sufficient from everything other than Him? And from it also is taken that it is not obligatory upon Him, exalted, to do anything from possible things nor to refrain from them; for if something from them were obligatory upon Him rationally, like recompense for example, He would be dependent upon that thing to be perfected by it; for nothing is obligatory in His right except what is perfection for Him, how could that be when He is the Self-Sufficient from everything other than Him? And as for the dependency of everything other than Him upon Him, it necessitates for Him, exalted, life, omnipotence, will, and knowledge; for if any of them were absent, it would not be possible for (anything) to exist...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage explains that the entire creed is encapsulated in the shahada. The author first defines 'al-uluhiyya' as two aspects: Allah's self-sufficiency (istighna') from all else, and the dependency (iftiqar) of all else upon Him. The shahada negates any self-sufficient besides Allah and affirms that all others depend on Him. Then he deduces the necessary attributes. From istighna', we derive: existence (wujud), pre-eternity (qidam), everlastingness (baqa'), dissimilarity to originated things (mukhalafa lil-hawadith), self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafs), and transcendence from deficiencies (tanzuh 'an al-naqa'is). Additionally, it implies the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech; otherwise, He would need something. It also implies His transcendence from accidents and that no action is obligatory upon Him. From iftiqar, we derive: life (hayat), omnipotence (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm); without these, nothing could exist. The passage is cut off at the end but clearly continues.

Attributes Necessitated by Self-Sufficiency and Dependency
Sanusi derives essential attributes from the concepts of istighna' and iftiqar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's self-sufficiency and the world's dependency necessitate specific divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are logically required by His nature as the Self-Sufficient and the One upon whom all depend.

From the two aspects of divinity—self-sufficiency and dependency—Sanusi deduces a list of necessary attributes. If Allah is truly self-sufficient, He must be eternal, unlike creation, and free from need. If everything depends on Him, He must have life, power, will, and knowledge. This logical derivation shows the systematic nature of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the structure
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the passage first discusses attributes from self-sufficiency, then from dependency. Underline the attributes listed.
The passage is cut off, but the logical flow is clear.
2
Identify the first set of attributes (from istighna')
Sanusi lists existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity, self-subsistence, and transcendence from deficiencies. Also included are hearing, sight, and speech. Ask: why are these necessary?
Without them, Allah would be in need of something external.
3
Understand the implications of istighna'
He further infers that Allah is not subject to accidents and that no action is obligatory upon Him. Reflect on how these follow from self-sufficiency.
Obligation would imply need for perfection from others.
4
Identify the second set of attributes (from iftiqar)
The dependency of all creation necessitates life, omnipotence, will, and knowledge. Without these, nothing could exist.
These are the attributes that enable creation to exist.

Oneness: The Implication of Dependence
Sanusi derives tawhid from the principle of dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of dependence (iftiqār) necessitates the oneness of Allah.

Core Idea
If there were two gods, nothing would depend on either; but everything depends on Allah.

Sanusi continues to draw consequences from the foundational principle that all created things depend on Allah. He now argues that this principle directly implies that Allah must be one. Read carefully how he shows that the existence of a second divinity would contradict the universal dependence of all things upon Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi uses a conditional argument: 'if there were a second... then...'
Look for the logical connective 'إذْ'.
2
Identify the argument
Identify the premise: everything depends on Allah. Then see how the hypothetical of a second god leads to a contradiction.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the impossibility of a second god follows from the necessity of universal dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيوجِبُ لَهُ أيضاً : الوحدانية؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الأَلوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ إليه شيءٌ لِلزومِ عَجزِهِما حِينَئِذٍ، كيْفَ وَهوَ الذِيَ يَفْتَقِرُ إِليهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ ويُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أيْضاً: حُدُوتُ العالم بأسْرِهِ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لكانَ ذَلكَ الشيءُ مُسْتغْنِياً عَنْهُ، تَعالى  َكيْفَ وَهُوَ الذِي يجبُ أَن يَفتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كل مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤخذُ منهُ أيْضاً أنه لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيءٍ مِنَ الكائنات في أثرِ ما، وإلا لزِمَ أَنْ يَستغني ذلِكَ الأثرُ عَنْ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ، كيفَ وَهُوَ يَفتقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عَمُوماً وعَلى كلِّ حَالٍ؟ هَذا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيئاً مِنَ الكائناتِ يُؤَثِرُ بِطبْعهِ. وأمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَتِراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلها اللهُ فِيهِ كما يَزْعَمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الجَهَلَة؛ فذلِكَ مُحالٌ أيضاً؛ لأَنَّهُ يَصيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ مفتقِراً في إيجادِ بَعْضِ الأفعالِ إلى واسِطةٍ، وذَلِكَ باطل لِما عَرَفْتَ مِنْ وُجُوبِ استغنائِهِ جلّ وَعَزّ عَنْ كُلّ مَا سِوَاهُ. فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ: لا إلهَ إلا الله لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ التي يَجِبُ عَلَى المكلَّف مَعْرِفتها فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جل وعزّ وَهِيَ : مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعالى، ومَا يَستَحيلُ، ومَا يَجوزُ.
And it also necessitates for Him: oneness (al-waḥdāniyyah); for if there were a second with Him in divinity, then nothing would depend on either of them due to the necessary occurrence of their impotence then — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him depends? And taken from it also is the temporal origination of the entire world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam bi-asrihī); for if something from it were eternal (qadīm), that thing would be self-sufficient from Him — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him must depend? And taken from it also is that there is no effect (taʾthīr) whatsoever from anything among created beings in any effect; otherwise, that effect would necessarily become self-sufficient from our Patron, glorified and mighty — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him depends generally and in every state? This is if you suppose that something among created beings has effect by its own nature. And as for if you suppose it to be effecting by a power that Allah placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, that is also impossible; because then our Patron, glorified and mighty, would become dependent in bringing about some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false due to what you have learned of the necessity of His self-sufficiency, glorified and mighty, from everything other than Him. So it has become clear to you that the statement 'there is no god but Allah' encompasses the three divisions which are obligatory for the morally responsible person to know regarding our Patron, glorified and mighty, and they are: what is necessary (al-wājib) in His right, exalted, what is impossible (al-mustaḥīl), and what is permissible (al-jāʾiz).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage continues to derive further implications from the principle of iftiqār (dependence). Sanusi derives three additional doctrines: (1) Oneness (tawḥīd): if there were two gods, neither could have full power, so nothing would depend on either; but since all things depend on Allah, there can be only one God. (2) The temporal origination of the entire world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam): if any part of the world were eternal, it would be self-sufficient and not dependent on Allah; but all things other than Allah depend on Him, so everything must have a beginning. (3) The negation of causality: no created thing can independently cause any effect, whether by its own nature or by a power Allah placed in it. If by nature, the effect would be independent of Allah; if by a power Allah placed, then Allah would be dependent on that intermediary, both contradicting His necessary self-sufficiency. Finally, Sanusi concludes that the entire discussion shows that the shahada encompasses the three categories of attributes every responsible person must know: necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and permissible (jāʾiz) in relation to Allah.

Key Terms
وحدانية
ألوهية
عجز
ثان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two gods could coexist if they share power.
Correction: Sanusi shows that any division of power results in impotence, making both incapable of being the ultimate object of dependence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would happen if there were two gods?
A) They would share power harmoniously
B) Nothing would depend on either due to their impotence
C) One would be the creator and the other not
D) All things would depend on both

Key Takeaway
The oneness of Allah is rationally necessitated because any second divinity would undermine the universal dependence of all creation upon Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every created thing depends on Allah alone, including your next breath, your income, and your family's safety, why do you still feel anxious about losing the support of people? What would it mean to truly depend on Allah alone, and what is one concrete step you can take today to shift that dependency?
?
The proof shows that any supposed second source of help would be impotent. Think of a person, institution, or possession you secretly rely on more than Allah. How does this passage expose the lie in that reliance? Are you willing to repent and redirect your trust?
?
You claim that Allah is one, but does your heart treat anything as a 'second' in terms of fear, hope, or love? Name one thing that competes with your devotion to Allah. What would it look like to uproot that idol from your heart today?

Temporal Origination: Everything Created Has a Beginning
Sanusi derives huduth al-alam from the necessity of dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the principle of dependence entails that the entire world (everything other than Allah) is temporally originated (hadith).

Core Idea
If anything were eternal, it would not need Allah; but everything needs Him.

Sanusi now applies the same reasoning to the entire universe. If any part of the world were eternal (qadim), it would be self-sufficient and not dependent on Allah. But we know everything other than Allah must depend on Him, so everything must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that Sanusi says 'the entire world' (bi-asrihi).
The argument mirrors the previous one on oneness.
2
Analyze the logic
Identify the hypothetical: if something from the world were eternal, then it would be self-sufficient. But that contradicts the premise that everything depends on Allah.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect on how the same principle of iftiqar yields a new conclusion about the nature of the world.
This is a classic argument for the creation of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيوجِبُ لَهُ أيضاً : الوحدانية؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الأَلوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ إليه شيءٌ لِلزومِ عَجزِهِما حِينَئِذٍ، كيْفَ وَهوَ الذِيَ يَفْتَقِرُ إِليهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ ويُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أيْضاً: حُدُوتُ العالم بأسْرِهِ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لكانَ ذَلكَ الشيءُ مُسْتغْنِياً عَنْهُ، تَعالى  َكيْفَ وَهُوَ الذِي يجبُ أَن يَفتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كل مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤخذُ منهُ أيْضاً أنه لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيءٍ مِنَ الكائنات في أثرِ ما، وإلا لزِمَ أَنْ يَستغني ذلِكَ الأثرُ عَنْ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ، كيفَ وَهُوَ يَفتقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عَمُوماً وعَلى كلِّ حَالٍ؟ هَذا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيئاً مِنَ الكائناتِ يُؤَثِرُ بِطبْعهِ. وأمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَتِراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلها اللهُ فِيهِ كما يَزْعَمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الجَهَلَة؛ فذلِكَ مُحالٌ أيضاً؛ لأَنَّهُ يَصيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ مفتقِراً في إيجادِ بَعْضِ الأفعالِ إلى واسِطةٍ، وذَلِكَ باطل لِما عَرَفْتَ مِنْ وُجُوبِ استغنائِهِ جلّ وَعَزّ عَنْ كُلّ مَا سِوَاهُ. فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ: لا إلهَ إلا الله لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ التي يَجِبُ عَلَى المكلَّف مَعْرِفتها فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جل وعزّ وَهِيَ : مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعالى، ومَا يَستَحيلُ، ومَا يَجوزُ.
And it also necessitates for Him: oneness (al-waḥdāniyyah); for if there were a second with Him in divinity, then nothing would depend on either of them due to the necessary occurrence of their impotence then — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him depends? And taken from it also is the temporal origination of the entire world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam bi-asrihī); for if something from it were eternal (qadīm), that thing would be self-sufficient from Him — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him must depend? And taken from it also is that there is no effect (taʾthīr) whatsoever from anything among created beings in any effect; otherwise, that effect would necessarily become self-sufficient from our Patron, glorified and mighty — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him depends generally and in every state? This is if you suppose that something among created beings has effect by its own nature. And as for if you suppose it to be effecting by a power that Allah placed in it, as many of the ignorant claim, that is also impossible; because then our Patron, glorified and mighty, would become dependent in bringing about some actions upon an intermediary, and that is false due to what you have learned of the necessity of His self-sufficiency, glorified and mighty, from everything other than Him. So it has become clear to you that the statement 'there is no god but Allah' encompasses the three divisions which are obligatory for the morally responsible person to know regarding our Patron, glorified and mighty, and they are: what is necessary (al-wājib) in His right, exalted, what is impossible (al-mustaḥīl), and what is permissible (al-jāʾiz).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage continues to derive further implications from the principle of iftiqār (dependence). Sanusi derives three additional doctrines: (1) Oneness (tawḥīd): if there were two gods, neither could have full power, so nothing would depend on either; but since all things depend on Allah, there can be only one God. (2) The temporal origination of the entire world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam): if any part of the world were eternal, it would be self-sufficient and not dependent on Allah; but all things other than Allah depend on Him, so everything must have a beginning. (3) The negation of causality: no created thing can independently cause any effect, whether by its own nature or by a power Allah placed in it. If by nature, the effect would be independent of Allah; if by a power Allah placed, then Allah would be dependent on that intermediary, both contradicting His necessary self-sufficiency. Finally, Sanusi concludes that the entire discussion shows that the shahada encompasses the three categories of attributes every responsible person must know: necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and permissible (jāʾiz) in relation to Allah.

Key Terms
حدوث
العالم
قديم
استغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi conclude about anything that would be eternal from the world?
A) It would be dependent on Allah in some way
B) It would be self-sufficient from Allah
C) It would be a partner to Allah
D) It would be a parent of creation

Key Takeaway
The temporal origination of the entire creation is a logical consequence of the fact that everything besides Allah depends on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that everything you rely on (your health, wealth, relationships) is dependent on Allah, why do you sometimes act as if they are self-sufficient? What would change today if you lived with the constant awareness that nothing around you is eternal but only Allah?
?
The argument shows that anything eternal would not need Allah. Examine your heart: are there any attachments or comforts you treat as 'eternal'—as if they will always be there without needing Allah? How can you shift that dependence back to Him today?
?
If every part of creation is originated and dependent, that means even your own existence is not self-sufficient. Does that dependence feel like a burden or a liberation? How will you thank Allah today for giving you existence every moment?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
No Created Being Has Independent Causal Efficacy
Sanusi refutes independent causality in creatures using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no created thing can independently cause any effect, based on the principle of dependence.

Core Idea
If created things caused effects independently, those effects would not need Allah — but everything needs Him.

Sanusi takes the principle of dependence to its most controversial conclusion: no created being has any causal efficacy (ta'thir) whatsoever. He considers two possible ways a creature might be thought to cause effects — by its own nature or by a power Allah placed in it — and shows both are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice Sanusi presents two scenarios: effect by nature and effect by a power from Allah.
2
Understand the first scenario
If a creature causes an effect by its own nature, then the effect would be independent of Allah. That contradicts universal dependence.
Note the rhetorical 'kayfa'.
3
Understand the second scenario
If a creature causes effects by a power Allah placed in it, then Allah would depend on that intermediary for some acts, contradicting His self-sufficiency.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Thus, all effects are directly from Allah; creatures are mere loci of Allah's action.
This is a strong statement of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيوجِبُ لَهُ أيضاً : الوحدانية؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الأَلوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ إليه شيءٌ لِلزومِ عَجزِهِما حِينَئِذٍ، كيْفَ وَهوَ الذِيَ يَفْتَقِرُ إِليهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟ ويُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ أيْضاً: حُدُوتُ العالم بأسْرِهِ؛ إذْ لَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْهُ قَدِيماً لكانَ ذَلكَ الشيءُ مُسْتغْنِياً عَنْهُ، تَعالى  َكيْفَ وَهُوَ الذِي يجبُ أَن يَفتَقِرَ إِلَيْهِ كل مَا سِوَاهُ؟ وَيُؤخذُ منهُ أيْضاً أنه لا تَأْثِيرَ لِشَيءٍ مِنَ الكائنات في أثرِ ما، وإلا لزِمَ أَنْ يَستغني ذلِكَ الأثرُ عَنْ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ، كيفَ وَهُوَ يَفتقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ عَمُوماً وعَلى كلِّ حَالٍ؟ هَذا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ أَنَّ شَيئاً مِنَ الكائناتِ يُؤَثِرُ بِطبْعهِ. وأمَّا إِنْ قَدَّرْتَهُ مُؤَتِراً بِقُوَّةٍ جَعَلها اللهُ فِيهِ كما يَزْعَمُهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الجَهَلَة؛ فذلِكَ مُحالٌ أيضاً؛ لأَنَّهُ يَصيرُ حِينَئِذٍ مَوْلانا جل وعزّ مفتقِراً في إيجادِ بَعْضِ الأفعالِ إلى واسِطةٍ، وذَلِكَ باطل لِما عَرَفْتَ مِنْ وُجُوبِ استغنائِهِ جلّ وَعَزّ عَنْ كُلّ مَا سِوَاهُ. فَقَدْ بَانَ لَكَ تَضَمُّنُ قَوْلِ: لا إلهَ إلا الله لِلأَقْسَامِ الثَّلاثَةِ التي يَجِبُ عَلَى المكلَّف مَعْرِفتها فِي حَقِّ مَوْلانَا جل وعزّ وَهِيَ : مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعالى، ومَا يَستَحيلُ، ومَا يَجوزُ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something good happens in your life, do you sincerely attribute it directly to Allah, or do you secretly credit your own skill, effort, or the means you used? What does Sanusi's argument demand you change in your heart?
?
Think of a moment you felt proud of an achievement. Did you pause to realize that even the ability to act was created by Allah moment by moment? If you truly believed no created thing has independent causal efficacy, how would your sense of pride be transformed?
?
If every effect—from the beating of your heart to the success of your work—is directly from Allah, then what does that mean for the times you complain about 'bad luck' or 'other people's actions'? Will you commit today to seeing all events as coming directly from Him, without secondary causes that lessen your reliance on Him?

The Shahada Encompasses the Three Necessary Divine Categories
Sanusi concludes that the shahada implies the three rational categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the testimony of faith (la ilaha illallah) contains the three rational judgments regarding Allah: necessary, impossible, and permissible.

Core Idea
The shahada is not just a statement; it logically implies the entire framework of tawhid.

Sanusi concludes the passage by showing that the entire discussion of oneness, creation, and causality leads back to the single statement 'there is no god but Allah'. This statement encompasses the three categories (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) that every believer must understand about Allah. This lesson reveals the depth of the shahada.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the final sentence of the source passage. Notice how Sanusi summarizes that the shahada contains the three divisions.
2
Understand the three categories
Identify that the three categories are: what is necessary for Allah (existence, oneness, etc.), what is impossible for Him (partners, weaknesses), and what is permissible for Him (creating, not creating).
These categories were defined earlier in the text.
3
Connect to the earlier lessons
Realize that the oneness argument falls under the impossible category (second god impossible), huduth under the permissible (creation is permissible), and refutation of causality under impossibility of dependence on intermediaries.
4
Appreciate the unity
See how the entire kalam project is built on understanding these three judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: Sanusi shows it implies a whole system of necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the statement 'la ilaha illallah' encompass?
A) Only the oneness of Allah
B) The three categories: necessary, impossible, permissible
C) The attributes of mercy and justice
D) The names of Allah

Key Takeaway
The testimony of faith contains the complete rational framework of tawhīd, demanding knowledge of what is necessary, impossible, and permissible concerning Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Basmala's Indication of Divine Causality
Explanation of the basmala's 'bi' when issued from Allah, indicating divine causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the basmala, when uttered by Allah, serves as a profound statement of divine causality and encompasses all beliefs.

Core Idea
The basmala’s 'bi' points to Allah as the sole cause of all existence.

This lesson explores a deeper layer of the basmala often overlooked: what it means when Allah Himself speaks it. The source passage explains that the 'bi' indicates that everything exists by Allah, linking directly to all articles of faith. Let's unpack this theological insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the author's argument in his own words.
Notice the contrast between 'if from Lord' and the ordinary estimation.
2
Understand the shift in meaning
The author explains that for Allah, the 'bi' does not estimate a missing verb; instead it directly means 'by Me was what was...'. Grasp how this changes the interpretation.
This is a key theological insight.
3
Connect to all beliefs
The passage states that this 'bi' indicates all beliefs. Reflect on why that is: if everything is by Allah, then all articles of faith are implied.
Consider how this relates to divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت صادرة من المولى؛ فليس التقدير على ذلك؛ لأن المعنى: بي كان ما كان، وبي يكون ما يكون، وحينئذ يكون في الباء إشارة إلى جميع العقائد؛ لأن المراد: بي وُجِدَ ما وُجِدَ، وبي يُوجَدُ ما يُوجَدُ، ولا يكون كذلك إلا من الكمال، وتنزَّه عن صفات النقصان كما ذكره بعضُ أئمة التفسير.
And as for when it (the basmala) is issued from the Lord, then the estimation is not upon that; because the meaning is: By Me was what was, and by Me will be what will be. Then at that point in the 'bi' there is an indication to all beliefs; because the intended meaning is: By Me was found what was found, and by Me is found what is found. And it is not thus except from [one] described with perfection and transcendent from attributes of deficiency, as mentioned by some leading exegetes.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of the basmala when it comes from Allah (as opposed to from creatures). The author states that when the basmala is uttered by Allah, the estimated verb is not as for creatures; rather the 'bi' indicates that all existence is by Allah. The phrase 'بي كان ما كان وبي يكون ما يكون' (By Me was what was, and by Me will be what will be) shows that all past and future events are by Allah's power. This 'bi' therefore refers to all the beliefs (العقائد) because it entails that everything exists by Allah. The author then adds that this can only be from one who is described with perfection and free from any deficiency, as some exegetes noted. This is a key kalam point: the basmala encapsulates tawhid and divine attributes.

Defending the Necessary: Conception vs. Assent
Al-Bajuri records the objection that even the necessary can be mentally conceived as absent, and answers by redefining tasawwur as tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Bajuri defends the definition of the necessary being (wajib) against the objection that it can be conceived as nonexistent, by distinguishing between pure conception (tasawwur) and rational assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Conception alone does not disprove necessity; only rational assent matters.

Have you ever thought about something that is logically impossible, like a square circle? The mind can picture it, but that doesn't make it real. In this passage, Al-Bajuri faces a similar challenge: someone objects that even the necessary being (God) can be mentally imagined as absent. His answer is subtle yet powerful—he redefines what 'conception' means in the definition of the necessary. Let's see how he turns the objection into a clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first part: the objector claims that the necessary can be conceived as non-existent because the mind can conceive impossibilities.
Note the word 'مُحال' (impossible) – the objector uses the mind's ability to think impossibilities as a premise.
2
Analyze the answer: redefining 'tasawwur'
Al-Bajuri answers that 'tasawwur' in the definition of necessary means 'tasdiq' (assent), not mere imagination. Understand the difference: assent implies intellectual acceptance, not just picturing.
The terms 'إذعان' (submission) and 'قبول' (acceptance) emphasize a cognitive commitment.
3
Identify the two types of necessary
The answer then distinguishes between necessary by necessity (self-evident) and necessary by inquiry (needs proof). See the example: a body occupying space is immediate; God's power requires reflection.
Both types are included because the mind cannot ultimately assent to their nonexistence.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Ask yourself: How does the redefinition of tasawwur neutralize the objection? Why is it important that even theoretical necessities (like God's power) are included?
The answer preserves the definition while acknowledging the mind's ability to imagine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ بأن الواجب قد يتصوَّر في العقل عَدَمُه؛ إذ العقل قد يتصوَّرُ المحال. وأجيب: بأن المراد بالتصور هنا التصديق، بمعنى: الإذعان والقبول. ودخل في التعريف كل من الواجب الضروري والواجب النظري، والأول: هو ما لا يحتاج إلى نَظَرِ واستدلال كالتحيز للجزم، بمعنى: أخذه قدراً من الفراغ الموهوم. والثاني: هو ما يحتاج إلى ذلك كقدرة الله تعالى، وكذا سائر ما ذكر في هذا الفن.
It was objected that the necessary being may be conceived in the mind as non-existent; because the mind may conceive the impossible. And it was answered: that what is meant by conception (taṣawwur) here is assent (taṣdīq), in the sense of acceptance (idhʿān) and approval (qabūl). And both the necessary by necessity (al-wājib al-ḍarūrī) and the necessary by theoretical inquiry (al-wājib al-naẓarī) are included in the definition. The first is that which does not require reflection (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl), like 'occupying space' (al-taḥayyuz) for a body (jizm), meaning taking it as a portion of imaginary void (al-farāgh al-mawhūm). The second is that which requires that, like the power of God (qudrat Allāh taʿālā), and likewise the rest of what is mentioned in this discipline.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of the necessary (wājib). The objector argues that even the necessary being can be mentally conceived as non-existent, because the mind is capable of conceiving impossible things. For example, one can imagine the non-existence of God's power. The answer reinterprets the term 'taṣawwur' (conception) used in the definition not as mere imaginative conception, but as 'taṣdīq' (assent), meaning intellectual acceptance and approval. This shifts the criterion: it is not that the mind cannot picture the non-existence of the necessary, but that the mind cannot affirm or accept its non-existence. The answer then distinguishes two types of necessary being: al-wājib al-ḍarūrī (the necessary by itself, self-evident) which requires no proof, such as a body's occupation of space—this is grasped immediately without reflection; and al-wājib al-naẓarī (the necessary by inquiry) which requires proof, such as the power of God. Both are included in the definition of wājib because the mind, after reflection, cannot assent to their non-existence. The passage also illustrates the first type with the example of a body occupying imaginary void (al-farāgh al-mawhūm), a standard kalām concept.

Key Terms
اعتراض
تصور
تصديق
إذعان
قبول
واجب ضروري
واجب نظري
تحيز
جزم
فراغ موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully refutes the definition of wājib because the mind can imagine its non-existence.
Correction: The objection only works if 'conception' means pure imagination; the answer clarifies that the definition uses 'conception' in the sense of rational assent, which cannot be given to the non-existence of the necessary.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection, what is meant by 'taṣawwur' in the definition of wājib?
A) Pure imagination (takhayyul).
B) Perception (idrāk).
C) Assent (taṣdīq) in the sense of acceptance.
D) Sensation (ḥiss).

Key Takeaway
The necessary being cannot be rationally denied, even if it can be imagined as absent, because the definition hinges on rational assent, not mere conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you merely conceive of His existence in your mind, or do you truly assent to it in your heart? What would change in your daily life if you moved from conception to full acceptance? Take one practical step today to affirm your faith through action.
?
The passage says some truths are self-evident and need no proof. Is your belief in God self-evident to you, or do you rely on arguments? Examine your heart: are you convinced deep down, or do you still entertain doubts? Write down one proof that strengthens your assent.
?
The objection arises when we confuse imagination with acceptance. Where in your life do you let the mere possibility of God's absence (or lack of evidence) trouble you? How can you train your mind to replace those imagined scenarios with rational assent? Commit to reading one reflective passage daily for a week.

Why al-Sanusi Did Not Prove Necessary Existence Directly
Al-Bajuri justifies al-Sanusi's decision not to prove necessary existence directly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sanusi's pedagogical reason for proving existence as such before proving necessary existence and its implications for teaching beginners.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi separated proofs to make learning easier, not because he doubted necessity.

When studying the divine attributes, one might expect that since existence is listed as necessary, the proof would be for necessary existence directly. However, al-Sanusi intentionally avoided that path. In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the teacher's wisdom behind that choice, showing how pedagogical concerns shape theological presentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice that al-Bajuri is justifying al-Sanusi's method.
Pay attention to the phrase 'كان مقتضى ما سلكه أوّلاً' which sets up the expectation.
2
Identify the two approaches
Al-Bajuri contrasts what might have been expected (proving necessary existence directly) with what al-Sanusi actually did (proving existence as such). Find these in the text.
3
Understand the 'excuse'
Al-Bajuri gives the author's excuse: proving necessary existence would automatically prove eternity and everlastingness, eliminating the need for separate proofs. This would deprive the beginner of a detailed exposition.
The word 'عذر' means excuse or justification.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical benefit
Consider why a detailed exposition (التفصيل) is 'closer to understanding'. Think about how breaking down a complex argument helps a new student.
Al-Bajuri uses 'أَقْرَبُ إلى الفهم' (closer to understanding).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وُجُودُهُ تَعَالَى كان مقتضى ما سلكه أوّلاً - حيث أخذ الوجود مقيداً بالوجوب؛ لأنه قال: (فمما يجب لمولانا جل وعز عشرون صفة وهي الوجود... إلخ) ـ أن يُبزهِنَ هنا على وجوب وجوده تعالى كما فعل بعض المتكلمين، لكن عُذْرُ المصنف: أنه لو بَرْهَنَ على وجوب الوجود؛ لم يحتج لإقامة البرهان على القدم والبقاء لتضمن وجوب الوجود لهما، فيفوتُ التفصيل الذي هو أَقْرَبُ إلى الفهم، فلذلك برهن على الوجود من حيث هو ثم أقام البرهان على القدم والبقاء تقريباً على المبتدئ. واعتُرضَ بأن البرهان الذي ذكره لا يدلُّ على وجوده تعالى، وإنما يدلُّ على وجود مُوجد، وأما كونه هو الله أو غيرُه؛ فلم يُسْتَفَد منه. وأجيب بأن هذا البرهان أفاد ذلك بواسطة ما وَرَدَ عن الرُّسُل من أن ذلك
His saying: 'His existence, exalted is He.' It was required by what he adopted initially—where he took existence as qualified by necessity; because he said: 'Among what is necessary for our Lord, mighty and majestic, are twenty attributes: existence... etc.'—that he prove here the necessity of His existence, exalted, as some theologians did. But the excuse of the author is that if he proved the necessity of existence, he would not need to establish the proof for eternity and everlastingness, because the necessity of existence implies them, so the detailed exposition which is closer to understanding would be lost. Therefore he proved existence as such, then established the proof for eternity and everlastingness to make it easier for the beginner. And it was objected that the proof he mentioned does not indicate His existence, exalted, but rather indicates the existence of a maker. As for whether it is Allah or something else, that is not derived from it. And it was answered that this proof provides that by means of what has been transmitted from the prophets that that...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of al-Sanusi's approach to proving God's existence. Al-Bajuri begins by noting that initially al-Sanusi listed existence among the necessary attributes of God, implying that existence is necessary. One might expect him to then prove that God's existence is necessary, as some others did. However, al-Sanusi chose not to do so, and al-Bajuri gives his excuse: if he proved necessary existence directly, that alone would also imply eternity and everlastingness, so there would be no need to prove them separately. That would deprive the beginner of the detailed exposition of each attribute, which is easier to understand. So he instead presents separate proofs for existence (as such), then eternity, then everlastingness. Next, an objection is raised: the proof for existence that al-Sanusi gives (the standard kalam cosmological argument from contingency) proves only the existence of a maker, not that this maker is specifically Allah (i.e., the one described in revelation). The answer is that the proof establishes a maker, and the identity of that maker as Allah is known through the reports of the prophets. Thus the proof is not self-sufficient in identifying the deity, which is a matter of faith in revelation.

Key Terms
برهان الوجود
وجوب الوجود
القدم
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Sanusi's omission of a direct proof for necessary existence indicates that he did not consider existence to be necessary or that he made an error.
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-Sanusi did believe in necessary existence but chose a pedagogical approach: proving necessary existence would make separate proofs for eternity and everlastingness redundant, so he intentionally separated them to help beginners understand each attribute in detail.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Sanusi choose not to prove the necessity of God's existence directly?
A) Because he thought existence is not necessary.
B) Because proving necessary existence would make separate proofs for eternity and everlastingness redundant, and he wanted a detailed exposition for beginners.
C) Because he did not believe in the necessity of existence.
D) Because he wanted to rely solely on revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi intentionally proved existence as such rather than necessary existence to preserve the detailed exposition of each attribute for beginners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah's attributes, do you settle for a general understanding or do you seek the detailed exposition that al-Sanusi preserved for beginners? What step will you take today to deepen your comprehension of one attribute?

?
The necessity of Allah's existence implies His eternity and everlastingness. Do you live as though the eternal, ever-lasting One is with you in every moment? How would your actions change if you truly felt that His necessity means He cannot be absent from your life?

The Objection: Proof Only Shows a Maker, Not Allah
Al-Bajuri presents and answers the objection that the proof only establishes a maker, not Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that al-Sanusi's cosmological argument only proves a maker, and the answer that prophets identify that maker as Allah.

Core Idea
Reason proves a maker; revelation identifies the maker as Allah.

After explaining al-Sanusi's proof for existence, al-Bajuri addresses a common objection: the proof only shows that there is a maker of the universe, but does it show that this maker is specifically Allah? This objection touches on the relationship between reason and revelation. The answer is that reason establishes the existence of a creator, and revelation tells us who that creator is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
The objection begins with 'واعتُرضَ' (and it was objected).
2
Analyze the objection
The objection states that the proof (al-Sanusi's cosmological argument) only indicates the existence of a maker (موجد), not that the maker is specifically Allah. This is a gap between reason and specific identity.
3
Understand the answer
The answer states that the proof does give knowledge of the maker, but the identification of that maker as Allah comes through what has been reported from the prophets (ما ورد عن الرسل). Thus reason and revelation work together.
The text says 'بواسطة ما ورد عن الرسل' (by means of what came from the prophets).
4
Reflect on the interplay
Consider how this answer maintains the validity of rational proof while acknowledging the necessity of revelation for complete theological knowledge.
This shows that kalam does not claim reason alone can identify God in all His specific attributes.
