Sanusi's Method: Why Prove Existence First?
Sanusi explains his choice to prove existence rather than necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's methodological reason for proving existence without qualification before proving pre-eternity and everlastingness.

Core Idea
Sanusi proves existence first to keep his proofs clear for beginners.

Sanusi could have directly proven God's necessary existence, which would automatically imply pre-eternity and everlastingness. However, he deliberately proved existence itself first, then separately proved pre-eternity and everlastingness. This passage explains his pedagogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to understand the flow.
2
Identify the 'excuse'
Find the reason Sanusi gives for not proving necessary existence directly.
The key is that proving necessary existence would merge multiple attributes.
3
Reflect on the pedagogical benefit
Think about why separating proofs helps beginners.
Sanusi wants detailed explanation (tafsil) for better understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وُجُودُهُ تَعَالَى كان مقتضى ما سلكه أوّلاً - حيث أخذ الوجود مقيداً بالوجوب؛ لأنه قال: (فمما يجب لمولانا جل وعز عشرون صفة وهي الوجود... إلخ) ـ أن يُبْرِهِنَ هنا على وجوب وجوده تعالى كما فعل بعض المتكلمين، لكن عُذْرُ المصنف: أنه لو بَرْهَنَ على وجوب الوجود؛ لم يحتج لإقامة البرهان على القدم والبقاء لتضمن وجوب الوجود لهما، فيفوتُ التفصيل الذي هو أَقْرَبُ إلى الفهم، فلذلك برهن على الوجود من حيث هو ثم أقام البرهان على القدم والبقاء تقريباً على المبتدئ. واعتُرضَ بأن البرهان الذي ذكره لا يدلُّ على وجوده تعالى، وإنما يدلُّ على وجود مُوجد، وأما كونه هو الله أو غيرُه؛ فلم يُسْتَفَد منه. وأجيب بأن هذا البرهان أفاد ذلك بواسطة ما وَرَدَ عن الرُّسُل من أن ذلك الموجد هو الله تعالى، فصح كونُ هذا البرهان دليلاً على وجوده تعالى، لكن مع الضميمة المذكورة.
His saying: 'His existence, may He be exalted, was the implication of what he followed initially – since he took existence restricted by necessity; because he said: (Among what is necessary for our Master, Glorified and Exalted, are twenty attributes, which are existence... etc.) – that he should prove here the necessity of His existence, Exalted be He, as some theologians did. But the excuse of the author: if he had proven the necessity of existence, he would not have needed to present the proof for pre-eternity and everlastingness because the necessity of existence includes them both, and thus the detailed explanation which is closer to understanding would be lost. Therefore he proved existence without qualification and then presented the proof for pre-eternity and everlastingness as a simplification for the beginner. And it was objected that the proof he mentioned does not indicate His existence, Exalted be He, but only indicates the existence of an originator. As for whether it is God or other than Him, that is not derived from it. And it was answered that this proof provided that by means of what was transmitted from the messengers, that that originator is God, Exalted be He. So it is correct that this proof is a proof for His existence, Exalted be He, but with the mentioned supplement.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of Sanusi's methodological choice in proving God's existence. It begins by noting that Sanusi initially presents existence as a necessary attribute (wājib), but in his proof he does not directly prove the necessity of existence (wujūb al-wujūd) as some theologians do. The commentator gives Sanusi's 'excuse': if he had proven necessary existence, that would automatically entail pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā'), and he would not need separate proofs for them. He deliberately proves existence without qualification first, then separately proves pre-eternity and everlastingness to provide a more detailed and accessible exposition for beginners. Then an objection is raised: the proof only shows that there is an originator (mūjid), not that it is God specifically. The answer is that the proof, combined with information transmitted from the messengers, confirms that the originator is God. Thus the proof is valid for demonstrating God's existence when supplemented by revelation. This guards against the misconception that rational proof alone can establish all divine attributes without recourse to transmitted sources.

Key Terms
وجود
وجوب الوجود
قدم
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Sanusi should have proven necessary existence directly as it is more efficient.
Correction: Sanusi intentionally avoided that to maintain clarity for beginners, as proving necessary existence would automatically include other attributes and lose the opportunity for separate detailed proofs.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Sanusi gives for proving existence without qualification before proving pre-eternity and everlastingness?
A) To avoid logical contradictions
B) To provide clearer exposition for beginners by not merging attributes
C) Because necessary existence cannot be proven
D) To follow the method of earlier theologians

Key Takeaway
Sanusi prioritizes pedagogical clarity by proving existence first and separately proving pre-eternity and everlastingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study the attributes of Allah, do you rush to grasp the conclusions, or do you patiently follow each step like Sanusi designed? Which approach truly settles your heart and deepens your certainty?
?
Sanusi deliberately took the longer path—proving existence first, then pre-eternity and everlastingness—so that beginners could understand. Is there an area of your faith where you have settled for a shallow understanding because you were too impatient for the detailed proofs? Will you commit to going back and slowly building that foundation?
?
The commentator notes that proving necessary existence would have been more efficient. But Sanusi chose clarity over efficiency for the sake of the learner. In your own learning and teaching of deen, do you prioritize being understood over being impressive? How can you emulate Sanusi's humility and care for the beginner today?

The author presents an objection and its answer regarding Sanusi's proof.

The Proof Needs Revelation to Identify God
Objection and answer about proof identifying God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational proof for a creator requires prophetic revelation to confirm that the creator is specifically Allah.

Core Idea
Reason points to a creator; revelation names Him.

A common objection to the cosmological proof is that it only shows there is an originator, not that this originator is God. The answer shows that rational theology and revelation work together: reason establishes that a creator exists, and the prophets tell us that this creator is Allah. This lesson explains the relationship between reason and revelation in identifying the divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims the proof only shows an originator (mūjid), not specifically God. Notice the distinction: the proof gives existence, not identity.
The objection is not denying the proof's value, only its sufficiency.
2
Understand the answer
The answer affirms that the proof, when combined with what the messengers brought, does indicate God. The rational proof is not standalone; it is supplemented by revelation.
The key phrase is 'bi-wāsiṭat mā warada 'an al-rusul'.
3
Reflect on the division of labor
Reason proves a creator; revelation names Him and gives His attributes. This prevents overreliance on reason alone in theology.
The passage also states that divine names are only known from revealed proofs (adilla shar'iyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرضَ بأن البرهان الذي ذكره لا يدلُّ على وجوده تعالى، وإنما يدلُّ على وجود مُوجد، وأما كونه هو الله أو غيرُه؛ فلم يُسْتَفَد منه. وأجيب بأن هذا البرهان أفاد ذلك بواسطة ما وَرَدَ عن الرُّسُل من أن ذلك الموجد هو الله تعالى، فصح كونُ هذا البرهان دليلاً على وجوده تعالى، لكن مع الضميمة المذكورة. (وقوله: وَأُجِيبَ... إلخ) حاصل الجواب أن تسمية ذلك الموجد بلفظ الجلالة، ومثله غيره من الأسماء إنما علم بواسطة الرسل، والمقصود من الدليل: إثبات موجد للعالم، ولا تعلم أسماؤه إلا من الأدلة الشرعية.
It was objected: the proof he mentioned does not indicate His existence, Exalted be He, but only indicates the existence of an originator. As for it being God or other than Him, that is not derived from it. And it was answered: this proof provided that by means of what was transmitted from the messengers, that that originator is God, Exalted be He. So it is correct that this proof is a proof for His existence, Exalted be He, but with the mentioned supplement. (And his saying: 'And it was answered... etc.') The summary of the answer is that naming that originator with the word of majesty (Allah) and similar other names is known only by means of the messengers, and the purpose of the proof is to establish an originator for the world, and His names are known only from the revealed proofs.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection to the cosmological proof for God's existence. The objector argues that Sanusi's proof only demonstrates the existence of an originator (mūjid) but does not identify that originator as God (Allah) as opposed to some other being. The answer is twofold. First, the rational proof combined with the messages of the prophets (al-rusul) confirms that the originator is God. Second, the commentator explains that naming that originator 'Allah' and other divine names are only known through prophetic revelation (shar'iyya). Thus, the rational proof establishes that there is a creator, and revelation identifies him as the God with specific names. This guards against the misunderstanding that rational theology alone can fully identify the divine essence without reliance on transmitted knowledge.

Resolving the Objection: Simple and Compound Proof
Objection and answer about the logical form of the proof from temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against Sanusi's proof from temporality is resolved by interpreting the proof either as a simple term (with the aspect of indication as temporality) or as a compound syllogism.

Core Idea
The proof is the world from the aspect of its temporality, not a bare term.

In his discussion of the world's temporality, Sanusi faces a logical objection: his wording seems to treat temporality itself as the proof, but that creates a dilemma. Either the proof is a simple term (the world) or a compound syllogism. This lesson unpacks the objection and the elegant two-part answer, which clarifies the role of the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalāla) and the implied major premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer with two possible interpretations.
The objection is a dilemma; the answer resolves both horns.
2
Understand the dilemma
The objector says: if the proof is a simple term, it should be 'the world', not 'temporality'; if it is a compound syllogism, Sanusi only mentions one premise. Identify why each horn seems problematic.
3
Examine the simple interpretation
The answer says we can take the proof as simple if we treat 'temporality' as the aspect of indication. Then 'temporality of the world' means 'the world from the aspect of its temporality'. This makes temporality the proof's effective element.
This introduces the key concept of jiha al-dalāla (aspect of indication).
4
Examine the compound interpretation
Alternatively, treat it as a syllogism: the minor premise is 'the world is temporally originated', and the major premise ('every temporally originated thing has an originator') is implied. Sanusi later mentions the proof of the major.
The phrase 'fa-ḥudūth al-ʿālam' is shorthand for the syllogism.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Notice that the answer also distinguishes temporality from possibility (imkān). This guards against confusing Sanusi's proof with the later kalam argument based on contingency (imkān).
The aspect of indication is specifically temporality, not possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فَحُدُوثُ الْعَالَمِ اعتُرضَ: بأنه إن جُعِلَ الدليل مفرداً؛ فهو العالم لا حدوثه، وإن جعل مركباً؛ فهو المركَّب من مقدمتين قائلتين: العالم حادث، وكلُّ حادث لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثٍ، وعلى كلّ؛ فكلامُهُ غير صحيح. وأجيب بأنه يمكن إجراؤه على الأول، لكن لما كان الحدوث هو جهة الدلالة؛ كان كأنه هو الدليل، وعلى هذا فالمعنى: فالعالَمُ من حيث حدوثه، وحينئذ ففي كلامه إشارة إلى أن جهة دلالة العالم على وجوده تعالى: هي حدوثه لا إمكانه مثلاً. ويمكن إجراؤُهُ على الثاني؛ لأن حدوث العالم في قوة الصغرى القائلة: العالم حادث، ولا بُدَّ من أن يَنْضَمَّ إليها الكبرى القائلة وكل حادث لا بُدَّ له من مُحْدِثٍ، فيكون قد أشار إلى الصغرى وحَذَفَ الكبرى، لكنه ذَكَرَ دليلها بقوله: (لأنه لو لم یکن له محدث... إلخ).
His saying: 'So the temporality of the world' – it was objected: if the proof is taken as simple (mufrad), then it is the world, not its temporality; and if taken as compound (murakkab), then it is the compound of two propositions: 'The world is temporally originated' and 'Every temporally originated thing necessarily has an originator'. In either case, his statement is not correct. And it was answered: it can be applied to the first, but since temporality (ḥudūth) is the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalāla), it is as if it is the proof. On this interpretation, the meaning is: 'the world from the aspect of its temporality'. And in that case, his statement indicates that the aspect of the world's indication of His existence, Exalted be He, is its temporality, not its possibility (imkān) for example. And it can be applied to the second, because the temporality of the world is in the position of the minor premise saying 'The world is temporally originated', and it must be joined by the major premise 'Every temporally originated thing necessarily has an originator'. So he indicated the minor premise and omitted the major, but he mentioned its proof in his saying: 'Because if it did not have an originator... etc.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses a logical objection about the formulation of Sanusi's proof based on the world's temporality (ḥudūth). The objector points out a dilemma: if the proof is considered a simple term (mufrad), then the proof is the world itself, not its temporality; but Sanusi's wording 'fa-ḥudūth al-ʿālam' suggests the temporality is the proof. If the proof is considered compound (murakkab) like a syllogism, then it should consist of two premises, but Sanusi only mentions one. The answer provides two compatible resolutions. First, on the simple interpretation, since temporality is the aspect (jiha) that indicates the existence of a creator, it is as if it is the proof. Thus the phrase means 'the world from the aspect of its temporality'. Second, on the compound interpretation, the temporality of the world functions as the minor premise, and the major premise ('Every temporally originated thing has an originator') is implied and later supported by Sanusi's statement about necessity of an originator. The commentator also notes that the aspect of indication is temporality, not possibility (imkān), which distinguishes the argument from the kalam proof based on contingency. This guards against confusion about whether the proof is the world itself or a logical argument.

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
جهة الدلالة (jiha al-dalāla)
دليل مفرد (dalīl mufrad)
دليل مركب (dalīl murakkab)
إمكان (imkān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is simply the phrase 'temporality of the world' as a single term, without any logical structure.
Correction: The answer shows that the phrase can be unpacked either as 'the world from the aspect of its temporality' (simple, with jiha) or as a full syllogism with an implied major premise.

Checkpoint
What is the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalāla) that makes the world a proof for God's existence according to this passage?
A) The world's possibility (imkān)
B) The world's temporality (ḥudūth)
C) The world's compound nature (tarkīb)
D) The world's pre-eternity (qidam)

Key Takeaway
The proof from temporality is not a bare term; it is the world considered under the aspect of its temporality, which can be expressed as a simple term or an implied syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you see it merely as existing, or do you perceive its 'temporality'—the fact that it began and depends on something else? How might this shift in perception change your daily gratitude or awe toward the Creator?

This lesson shows that the proof from temporality can be seen as a simple term or a syllogism. Which of these two formulations resonates more with how you instinctively recognize God's existence? What does your preference reveal about how you think about faith?
?
If the aspect of the world that points to God is its temporality rather than its possibility, what does that imply about your own life? Are you living as if your existence is self-sufficient, or are you constantly aware that each moment is a new creation from Him?

Motion and Rest Include Potential States – Al-Ajhuri's Defense
Al-Ajhuri defends the universality of motion and rest by including potential states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ajhuri defends the universality of motion and rest in bodies by including potential states.

Core Idea
Every body is either moving or still – actually or potentially.

In this lesson, we examine al-Ajhuri's marginal note on Umm al-Barahin. He anticipates an objection that some bodies seem to be always moving or always still, which would break the rule that all bodies are characterized by motion or rest. His answer is subtle: motion and rest include both actual and potential states. This preserves the universal applicability of the attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
The objection claims that some bodies (like mountains) are always still and some (like celestial bodies) are always moving, and others are unknown. This seems to contradict the claim that every body is subject to motion and rest.
Notice that the objection focuses on apparent exceptions.
2
Understand al-Ajhuri's solution: potentiality
Al-Ajhuri says that 'motion and rest' include those that exist actually (bil-fi'l) and those that exist potentially (bil-quwwa). So a mountain has potential motion even if actually still.
This distinction is crucial in kalam reasoning.
3
Reflect on the implication
With potential included, every body is indeed either in motion or rest (actually or potentially). There are no true exceptions. This shows how careful definitions are needed in theological arguments.
This lesson connects to the broader theme of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونِ ولا يرد على ذلك أن من الأجرام ما هو متحرك دائماً وما هو ساكن دائماً كالجبال، وأن منها ما لا يعلم حاله كالأجرام التي تحت الأرض؛ لأن المراد بالحركة والسكون ما يشمل الحاصلين بالفعل والحاصلين بالقوة، وما ذكر لا يخرج عنهما بعد كون المراد ما ذكر
Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'from motion and rest'. It is not objected against that that some bodies are perpetually moving and some are perpetually still like mountains, and that some of them have unknown states like bodies under the earth; because what is meant by motion and rest is what includes both those that exist actually and those that exist potentially, and what was mentioned does not fall outside them given that what is meant is what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is a marginal note from al-Ajhuri defending the claim that motion and rest are necessary concomitants of bodies. He anticipates an objection: if motion and rest are always necessary, then what about bodies that appear to be always moving (like celestial bodies) or always still (like mountains)? Also, some bodies are unknown, like those underground. Al-Ajhuri answers that the intended meaning of motion and rest includes both actual and potential states. So a mountain is potentially capable of motion even if actually still. Therefore, every body is either in motion or rest actually or potentially. This preserves the universality of the claim.

Key Terms
بالفعل
بالقوة
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest only refer to actual states.
Correction: In kalam, motion and rest include potential states, so every body is still subject to them.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, how does the inclusion of potential states resolve the objection?
A) It shows some bodies are exempt.
B) It makes the claim universally applicable.
C) It denies that mountains are still.
D) It only applies to known bodies.

Key Takeaway
Every body is either actually or potentially in motion or rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own life, are you more aware of your actual movements and stillness, or do you also consider the countless potential states? How does this expanded awareness affect your sense of gratitude for Allah's control over all possibilities? Commit this week to pause and recognize one potential blessing that became actual.
?
The passage teaches that even bodies with unknown states are covered by the rule. What hidden aspects of your own soul does Allah know fully, though you may be unaware? How does this motivate you to seek His guidance in areas of potential growth? This week, ask Him in sujood to reveal one potential weakness so you can work on it.
?
If every body is always either actually or potentially moving or resting, then nothing is ever truly independent. How does this truth challenge your sense of self-reliance? Will you consciously attribute every movement and stillness to Allah today, and what first step will you take in that moment?

Proof of the Temporality of Accidents and Response to the Objection
Al-Bajuri presents the proof from observed change and answers the objection that change is i'tibari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof for the temporality of accidents based on observed change and how scholars respond to the objection that change is merely a conceptual (i'tibari) relation.

Core Idea
Change is observed in accidents themselves, not as a separate entity.

Al-Bajuri presents one of the foundational proofs in kalam: since accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence, they must be temporal. But an objection arises: change is a relational concept, not an actual existing thing, so how can it be observed? This lesson walks through the proof, the objection, and the clever response that the wording is a loose expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the passage where al-Bajuri states the observation of accident change and the conclusion of temporality.
Notice the logical structure: observation → change → temporality.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims change is i'tibari and not observable. Grasp why this seems to break the proof.
Distinguish between an existent thing and a relation.
3
See the answer
Al-Bajuri answers that the wording is loose; the real observation is of the changing accidents themselves.
The clarification about motion/rest shows the nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِ الْأَعْرَاضِ مُشَاهَدَةُ تَغَيرِهَا... إلخ) تقريره هكذا: الأعراضُ شُوهِدَ تغيرها من وجود إلى عَدَمٍ وعكسه، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ونتيجته الأعراض حادثة. فإن قيل: التغيرُ أمر اعتباريّ لا تتعلَّق به المشاهَدَة؛ لأنها لا تتعلَّق إلا بالأمر الوجودي. أجيب بأن في العبارة تساهلاً والمراد أن الأعراض شوهِدَتْ متغيّرَةً. حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية وجود إلى عدم وعكسه، فقول المصنف : مشاهدة تغيرها.. إلخ) أي مشاهدتها متغيّرةً، لكن هذا لا يَظْهَرُ إلا في الوجودي منها كالسواد والبياض، دون نحو الحركة والسكون؛ لأن ذلك لا يشاهد، وإنما يشاهَدُ الجِزْمُ حال كونه متحركاً أوساكناً أو نحو ذلك، لكن لما ضاقَتْ عليهم العبارة تساهلوا في ذلك كما يُؤْخَذُ من كلام بعض من كتب على السكتاني.
His saying: 'And the proof of the temporality of accidents is the observation of their change... etc.' Its exposition is as follows: Accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and its opposite, and everything that is like that is temporal, and the conclusion is that accidents are temporal. If it is said: Change is an i'tibari matter to which observation does not pertain, because observation only pertains to existential matters. The answer is that in the expression there is looseness (tasahul), and the intended meaning is that accidents are observed as changing. The commentary of al-Bajuri on the Sunusiyya: existence to non-existence and its opposite, so the author's saying 'observation of their change' etc. means observing them as changing. But this is only apparent in existential ones like blackness and whiteness, not in the case of motion and rest, because those are not observed; rather, the body is observed as being moving or still or the like. But because the expression was too restrictive for them, they were lenient in that, as is taken from the words of some who wrote on al-Saktani.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a proof combined with an objection and answer. Al-Bajuri first states the proof: accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and anything that changes is temporal (since it wasn't before). Therefore accidents are temporal. The objection: change is a relational concept (i'tibari), not an existing thing, so observation cannot apply to it because observation only applies to existent things. The answer: there is a looseness in the wording; the real meaning is that we observe the accidents themselves as changing (e.g., we see blackness become whiteness). Then al-Bajuri further clarifies: this observation of change is only clear for existential accidents like colors; for motion and rest, they are not directly observed changing. Instead, we observe the body itself moving or being still. But due to the difficulty of expression, scholars allowed this loose phrasing, as others like al-Saktani noted.

Key Terms
دليل حدوث الأعراض
مشاهدة
تغير
اعتباري
تساهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change itself is an existential thing that can be observed directly.
Correction: Change is a relational concept; what is observed is the accident undergoing change, not change as an entity.

Checkpoint
How does al-Bajuri answer the objection that change is i'tibari and not observable?
A) He says change is existential.
B) He says the wording is loose and what is meant is that accidents are observed changing.
C) He says motion and rest are not accidents.
D) He says observation is not needed.

Key Takeaway
The proof for temporality of accidents relies on observing accidents themselves changing, not change as an entity; the wording is a concession for fluency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in Allah's existence based on reasoning, but do you actually observe the change in your own self? When you feel anger then calm, do you recognise that as proof of your own temporality, or do you live as if you are permanent? What would change if you truly lived with the awareness that you are changing every moment?
?
The scholars allowed looseness in wording for fluency in presenting proofs. In your conversations about faith, do you care more about precise truth or about making a point quickly? Where have you sacrificed accuracy for ease, and how can you repent from that?
?
This passage shows that observation of change is indirect for motion and rest. Consider your own spiritual state: do you observe your own heart changing from belief to doubt and back? Or do you assume you are permanently on one state? What would it take to honestly observe your own spiritual changes?

The Three Dependencies of the Proof
Al-Bajuri enumerates the three dependencies and refutes the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential pillars of the kalam proof for the temporality of bodies and how the first pillar is established.

Core Idea
The proof for bodies' temporality rests on three indispensable steps.

Al-Bajuri begins by laying out the complete structure of the proof. He shows that theologians must prove three things: that bodies have accidents, that accidents are inseparable from bodies, and that the chain of accidents cannot be beginningless. This first part introduces all three steps and then walks through the first objection, where the opponent denies that bodies have accidents at all. The refutation is immediate: direct observation confirms that every body has attributes like color or motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noticing the three dependencies listed in the first line. Then read the translation.
The phrase 'يتوقف على' indicates the proof's dependence.
2
Identify the three dependencies
List the three things the proof depends on: establishing accidents, establishing concomitance, and refuting beginningless accidents.
These form the backbone of the entire argument.
3
Understand the first objection
See how the opponent denies that bodies have any additional thing (accidents). The refutation is based on universal human perception.
Al-Bajuri emphasizes that even a rational person perceives accidents in themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها، وهو الأعراض، وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول: لا نسلّم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فتبطله بالمشاهدة؛ إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويُحِسُّ أن لذاته شيئاً زائداً عليها. فيقول: سلَّمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلّمُ الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام قنبطلُه بمشاهَدَةِ عدم الانفكاك. فيقول: سلَّمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلّم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام؛ لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة، وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها؛ إذ ما من حركة إلا وقَبْلَها حركة، وهكذا، فتكون حادثةً بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديمٌ، وشخصها حادث، فنبطله بأمور منها: أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شَخْصِه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً؛ لَزِمَ أن يكون النوع كذلك، فبَطَلَ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها.
And know that the proof of the temporality of bodies depends on establishing something additional to them, which is accidents; and on establishing the concomitance between them; and on refuting [the possibility of] accidents with no beginning. That is because the opponent may say: We do not concede that there is something additional to bodies. So you refute this by observation, for there is no rational person who does not perceive that his essence has something additional to it. Then he says: We concede that, but we do not concede the concomitance between it and bodies. So you refute it by observation of non-separation. Then he says: We concede that, but we do not concede that it indicates the temporality of bodies; because they might be eternal, and that additional thing is events with no beginning; for there is no movement except before it movement, and so on. Thus they are temporal in individual but eternal in type, meaning that the type of movement is eternal, while its individual is temporal. So you refute it by matters including that the type has no existence except within its individual; so if the individual is temporal, the type must also be temporal. Thus [the possibility of] events with no beginning is invalidated.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the complete logical structure of the kalam argument for the temporality of bodies (ajsam). Al-Bajuri states that the proof depends on three steps: first, establishing that bodies have accidents (zā’id) second, establishing that these accidents are necessarily concomitant with bodies (mulāzama); and third, refuting the possibility that the accidents are beginningless (hawādith lā awwal lahā). He then walks through the dialectical exchange with an opponent. The opponent first denies that bodies have accidents; this is refuted by observation: everyone perceives that their body has something additional like color or motion. The opponent then concedes accidents but denies they are inseparable; this is refuted by observing that bodies are never without accidents. The opponent then concedes accidents are inseparable but argues that bodies could be eternal and the accidents are an endless chain of events with no first occurrence, so bodies are eternal while accidents are temporal in their individuals but eternal as a type. Al-Bajuri refutes this by noting that a type exists only in its individuals; if every individual accident is temporal, the type must also be temporal. Thus the chain cannot be beginningless.

Key Terms
دليل حدوث الأجرام
أعراض
ملازمة
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does al-Bajuri refute the opponent's denial that bodies have accidents?
A) By quoting the Quran
B) By logical deduction
C) By observation (mushāhada)
D) By consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The proof for the temporality of bodies has three essential pillars, with the first being the existence of accidents established by direct observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof begins with undeniable observation: you perceive something additional to your own essence. Have you ever stopped to reflect that your own color, movement, and rest are not you but are accidents that come and go? If your body is constantly changing, what does that say about your own permanence?
?
The opponent denies accidents are additional to bodies. In your daily life, do you ever act as if your physical attributes (health, youth, beauty) are your very self rather than temporary gifts? How might taking this proof seriously change the way you attach to them?
?
The proof depends on accepting that accidents cannot be beginningless. Consider your own life: every aspect of your existence has a beginning. Does this help you see that you owe your entire being to a Creator who is eternal? What would it take for you to fully submit to this truth?

Establishing the Concomitance of Accidents
Al-Bajuri refutes the denial of concomitance by appealing to observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second pillar of the proof is established by observing that bodies never separate from accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies are never found without accidents; this inseparability proves the second pillar.

After conceding that bodies have accidents, the opponent may deny that accidents are necessarily connected to bodies. This lesson examines al-Bajuri's refutation: empirical observation shows that bodies are never free from accidents. This simple yet powerful point secures the second dependency of the proof and moves the argument forward.

Guided Path
1
Read the short Arabic passage
Read the opponent's concession and the refutation based on 'observation of non-separation'.
The phrase 'مشاهَدَةِ عدم الانفكاك' is key.
2
Understand the opponent's position
The opponent admits accidents exist but argues they could be separable from bodies.
3
Grasp the refutation
Al-Bajuri argues that we never observe a body without accidents; thus concomitance is empirically proven.
This is a direct appeal to universal experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting Beginningless Accidents
Al-Bajuri refutes the claim of beginningless accidents by showing type depends on individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument for beginningless accidents and al-Bajuri's refutation based on the dependence of type on individuals.

Core Idea
If every accident is temporal, the type cannot be eternal; thus the chain must have a beginning.

The opponent makes a sophisticated final objection: bodies might be eternal while accidents are an infinite series of events with no first occurrence. This would mean accidents are 'temporal in individual but eternal in type'. Al-Bajuri refutes this by pointing out that a type (e.g., motion) has no existence apart from its individual instances. If every individual accident is temporal, the type itself must be temporal. This crucial insight invalidates the possibility of beginningless accidents and completes the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the opponent's claim that accidents could be beginningless as a chain, with each individual temporal but the type eternal.
This is the most challenging part.
2
Understand the refutation
Al-Bajuri argues that the type exists only within individuals; if all individuals are temporal, the type is temporal.
This refutes the possibility of beginningless accidents.
3
Connect to the proof's completion
With this refutation, all three pillars are established, so bodies must be temporal.
The proof is now complete.

If every movement you make is part of a chain that must have a first cause, how does that realization reshape your awareness of Allah's presence in your daily actions? What one movement today will you dedicate consciously to Him?
?
The opponent mistakenly thought an abstract type could be eternal without concrete individuals. Are there abstract goals in your life—like 'being a good Muslim'—that you treat as eternal ideals while neglecting the specific deeds that give them reality? What specific action will you take today to close that gap?
?
Al-Bajuri's refutation shows that a type depends entirely on its individuals. Your faith (iman) is a type—does it exist only as a label, or is it realized through your individual acts of obedience? What is one act of worship you have been postponing that would make your iman truly exist in this moment?

The Four Refutations for Accidents' Temporality
Al-Bajuri lists the four refutations needed for the proof of accidents' temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four refutations that underpin the proof of the temporality of accidents in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The proof of accidents' temporality stands on refuting four possibilities.

Al-Bajuri presents a cornerstone of kalam: the argument for the temporality of accidents. Before the proof can be accepted, four objections must be neutralized. This passage lists them succinctly, showing the logical foundation of the entire cosmological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a list of four refutations followed by an example.
The Arabic uses the verbal noun 'ibṭāl' for each refutation.
2
Identify the four refutations
Work through each refutation one by one. Understand what each means: self-subsistence, transference, concealment, and the eternal perishing.
These correspond to four ways an opponent could deny change.
3
Connect to the example
Al-Bajuri gives an example of motion after rest. The opponent might claim motion existed before. This relates to the refutation of hawādith lā awwal lahā (beginningless events).
4
Ask: why these four?
Consider why these four refutations are necessary. They block all alternative explanations for observable change, leaving temporality as the only possibility.
This is the core of the argument's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدوث الأعراض يتوقف على إبطال قيام العَرَضِ بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله لغيره، وإبطال كونه، وإبطالِ أن القديمَ يَنْعَدِمُ، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يَمْنَعُ أنها تتغيَّرُ من عَدَمٍ إلى وجود وعكسه، فالحركة بعد السكون مثلاً لم تكن معدومةً ثم وجدَتْ، بل كانت موجودة قبل ذلك.
And the proof of the temporality of accidents depends on refuting that an accident subsists by itself, and refuting its transference to another, and refuting its concealment, and refuting that the eternal perishes. That is because the opponent may deny that they change from non-existence to existence and vice versa, as for example motion after rest was not non-existent and then came into existence, but rather existed before that.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This short passage enumerates the refutations that underpin the proof of the temporality of accidents. Al-Bajuri states that the proof depends on four refutations: (1) that an accident could subsist by itself (qiyām bi nafsihi), meaning independent of a body; (2) that it could transfer from one body to another; (3) that it could be concealed (kawn) within the body when its opposite appears; and (4) that the eternal (qadīm) could perish. These are potential objections an opponent might raise. The example given: the opponent might say that motion after rest was not non-existent and then created, but was always existing in some form—this implies a beginningless chain, which ties back to the refutation of hawādith lā awwal lahā. The four refutations ensure that the only possible explanation for observed change is temporality.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
انتقال
كون
انعدام القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accidents could be eternal or preexist in some form, thus not truly coming into existence from non-existence.
Correction: The four refutations show that accidents cannot subsist by themselves, transfer, be concealed, or be eternal and perish; the only remaining possibility is that they are temporal.

Checkpoint

B) Three.
C) Four.
D) Five.

Key Takeaway
The proof of accidents' temporality hinges on refuting these four possibilities, leaving temporality as the only coherent explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would viewing each change as a temporal accident created by Allah reshape your gratitude and reliance on Him? What is one change today you could consciously attribute to Allah's direct power?
?
The fourth refutation guards against the idea that the eternal could perish. How does this strengthen your certainty that Allah, the Eternal, is unchanging and ever-present? In what area of your life do you need to trust in His eternal nature more than your fleeting circumstances? What step can you take today to anchor your heart in His permanence?
?
This proof relies on refuting alternative explanations. Is there a belief about Allah or His creation that you hold without having first refuted the alternatives? Are you comfortable in your faith without having wrestled with doubts? What specific objection to the temporality of accidents have you perhaps neglected, and how will you engage with it honestly?

The Three Prerequisites for Proving the Creation of Bodies
Bajuri enumerates the three prerequisites for proving bodies are created and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical prerequisites required to prove that bodies are created, and how Kalam responds to common objections.

Core Idea
Bodies are proven created through accidents, their inseparability, and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Bajuri begins by explaining that proving the creation of bodies is not a straightforward observational claim; it requires establishing three prior points. These points form the backbone of the classic Kalam argument for the temporal origination of the universe. In this passage, he also anticipates and refutes objections from an opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the three prerequisites, then presents a dialogue with an opponent.
Pay attention to the logical flow from one prerequisite to the next.
2
Identify the three prerequisites
The passage lists three things the proof depends on: (1) establishing accidents (a'rad), (2) establishing inseparability (mulazama) between bodies and accidents, and (3) refuting events without beginning (hawadith la awwala laha). Find each in the text.
These are the three muqaddimat or premises of the argument.
3
Trace the opponent's objections
The opponent raises three denials corresponding to each prerequisite. Follow how the author refutes each one using observation (mushahada) or logical reasoning.
The first two refutations rely on empirical observation; the third uses a logical argument about type and individual.
4
Understand the refutation of eternal types
The opponent concedes accidents but claims they could be an infinite series, each preceded by another, making the type eternal though individuals are originated. The author refutes this by noting that a type has no existence apart from its individuals; if each individual is originated, the type must also be originated.
This refutes the possibility of an infinite regress through the dependence of type on individual instances.
5
Connect to the broader argument
This argument is not an isolated proof; it is the standard Kalam cosmological argument (Dalil al-Huduth). Understanding it helps grasp how theologians prove the existence of a Creator who brought the universe into being.
Reflect on why these three steps are necessary: they block every possible escape route for the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن دليل حدوث الأجرام يتوقف على إثبات زائد عليها، وهو الأعراض، وعلى إثبات الملازمة بينهما، وعلى إبطال حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها، وذلك لأن الخصم ربما يقول: لا نسلّم أن هناك زائداً على الأجرام، فتبطله بالمشاهدة؛ إذ ما من عاقل إلا ويُحِسُّ أن لذاته شيئاً زائداً عليها. فيقول: سلَّمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلّمُ الملازمة بينه وبين الأجرام فنبطلُه بمشاهَدَةِ عدم الانفكاك. فيقول: سلَّمنا ذلك، لكن لا نسلّم دلالته على حدوث الأجرام؛ لاحتمال أن تكون قديمة، وذلك الزائد حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها؛ إذ ما من حركة إلا وقَبْلَها حركة، وهكذا، فتكون حادثةً بالشخص قديمة بالنوع، بمعنى أن نوع الحركة قديمٌ، وشخصها حادث، فنبطله بأمور منها: أنه لا وجود للنوع إلا في ضمن شَخْصِه، فإذا كان الشخص حادثاً؛ لَزِمَ أن يكون النوع كذلك، فبَطَلَ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها.
Know that the proof of the creation of bodies depends on establishing something additional to them, namely accidents, and on establishing the inseparability between them, and on refuting events without beginning. That is because the opponent may say: we do not accept that there is something additional to bodies. So you refute it by observation, for there is no rational person but perceives that for his essence there is something additional to it. Then he says: we grant that, but we do not accept the inseparability between it and bodies. So we refute it by observing the lack of separation. Then he says: we grant that, but we do not accept that it indicates the creation of bodies, because it is possible that they are eternal and that additional thing is events without beginning, for there is no movement except that before it is a movement, and so on, so it is created in terms of individual but eternal in terms of type, meaning that the type of movement is eternal and its individual is originated. So we refute it by matters including that the type has no existence except within its individual; if the individual is originated, then the type must be likewise, so events without beginning are invalidated.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument proving the creation (huduth) of bodies (ajsam). The author first states that the proof requires establishing three things: (1) the existence of accidents (a'rad) in bodies, (2) the inseparability (mulazama) between bodies and accidents, and (3) the impossibility of accidents existing without a beginning (i.e., an infinite regress). 

The author then presents a hypothetical dialogue with an opponent. The opponent first denies that there is anything additional to bodies beyond their substance. The author refutes this by observation (mushahada): every rational person intuitively perceives that bodies have accidents like color and motion. The opponent concedes but denies the inseparability—claiming that bodies might exist without accidents at some point. The author counters by observing that we never see a body free from accidents. The opponent then concedes but argues that bodies could be eternal, with accidents being a series of events without a beginning—for example, each movement is preceded by another movement, so while individual events are originated, the type (like motion) is eternal. The author refutes this by pointing out that the type has no existence apart from its individual instances; if each individual accident is originated, the type must also be originated, thus dismantling the notion of an eternal type. This is a classic Kalam argument, often used to establish the temporality of the universe.

Key Terms
حدوث الأجرام
أعراض
ملازمة
حوادث لا أول لها
حادثة بالشخص قديمة بالنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies could be eternal if accidents are an infinite series without a beginning (type eternal, individuals originated).
Correction: The type (naw') has no existence except within its individual instances; if each individual accident is originated (hadith), the type must also be originated, making an infinite regress impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three prerequisites (muqaddimat) for proving the creation of bodies?
A) Establishing accidents, inseparability, and refuting events without beginning.
B) Establishing substance, accidents, and the eternity of the world.
C) Establishing the existence of God, miracles, and the Quran.
D) Establishing the soul, the body, and their interaction.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the creation of bodies rests on establishing accidents, their inseparability from bodies, and the impossibility of an infinite series of accidents, thereby refuting the notion that bodies could be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In the dialectic, the opponent concedes step by step. Are there steps in your own conviction about the Creator that you have not fully examined? What would it take to honestly face them without evasion?
?
Bajuri appeals to universal rational perception. When have you ignored what your own mind clearly perceives about the Creator because of an emotional or habitual attachment? Will you commit today to aligning your reasoning with the truth you already know?

The Three-Syllogism Proof for God's Pre-eternity
Al-Bajuri explains the three-syllogism proof for God's pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-syllogism proof for God's pre-eternity (qidam) and why circularity and infinite regress are impossible.

Core Idea
The impossibility of circularity and infinite regress forces the conclusion that God must be pre-eternal.

In this passage, al-Bajuri unpacks the logical proof for God's pre-eternity, showing that denying it leads to absurd consequences. He carefully structures the argument into three syllogisms, each building on the previous, to demonstrate that the only consistent position is that God is pre-eternal without beginning. This lesson will guide you through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read for overall flow, then for detail. Pay attention to the structure of the three syllogisms.
2
Identify the three syllogisms
The proof is built from three conditional statements. Notice how the conclusion of one becomes the premise of the next.
3
Understand the justification of each step
The commentator explains why each implication holds, e.g., why temporality implies need for a creator.
4
Grasp circularity and infinite regress
These two impossibilities are the key to the proof. Read the examples carefully to see why they are invalid.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The proof works backward: since the consequences are impossible, the original premise (that God is not pre-eternal) is false, so His pre-eternity is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ... إلخ) هذا البرهان لا يتم إلا بثلاثة أقيسة، ونَظْمُها هكذا لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، لكن كَوْنُه حادثاً محال؛ إذ لو كان حادثاً لافْتَقَرَ إلى مُحْدِثٍ، لكن افتقارَهُ إلى محدث محال؛ إذ لو افتقر إلى مُحْدِث للزم الدَّوْرُ أو التسلسل، وهما محالان. والأسهل في ترتيب اللوازم أن تقول لو لم يكن قديماً لكان حادثاً، ولو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحْدِث، ولو افتقر إلى محدث للزم الدورُ أو التسلسل، وهما محالان، فما أدى إليهما - وهو افتقاره إلى مُحْدِثٍ - مُحَالٌ، فما أدى إليه ـ وهو كونه حادثاً - محال، فما أدى إليه - وهو عدم كونه قديماً ـ محال، فثبت نقيضه، وهو المطلوب. ويَقْرُبُ من هذا صنيعُ المتن حيث اقتصر في ترتيب اللوازم على الوجه الذي ذكره. فتدبر. (قوله: لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ حَادِثاً ووجه التلازم بين المقدم والتالي: أن موجودٍ مُنْحَصِرٌ في القديم والحادث، فمتى لم يكن قديماً كان حادثاً. (قوله: فَيُفَتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثِ) أي: لأنه لا يَصِحُ أن يكون حَدَثَ بنفسه، وإلا لَزِمَ أن يكون أحد الأمرين المتساويين مساوياً لصاحبه راجحاً عليه بلا سبب، وهو محال؛ لما فيه من اجتماع المساواة والرُّجحان كما تقدم. (قوله: فَيَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ أَوِ التَّسَلْسُلُ أي: لأنه إذا افتقر إلى مُحْدِثٍ؛ لزم أن يفتقر مُحْدِثُه أيضاً إلى محدث لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، ثم إن تناهَتِ المُحْدَثون؛ لَزِمَ الدور: وهو توقف شيء على شيءٍ توقف عليه، كما لو فُرِضَ أن زيداً أحدث عمراً، . حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: فَلِأَنَّهُ) أي: فيقال في بيانه القدم واجب؛ لأنه، ولو قال: فهو أنه لو لم يكن... إلخ بجعل الضميرين عائدين على البرهان؛ لكان أوضح وهكذا يقال في جميع ما يأتي؛ لأن المقصود بيان نفس البرهان. (قوله: كَمَا لَوْ فُرِضَ أَنَّ زَيْداً... إلخ) ومثل ذلك ما لو أوجد زيد عمراً، وأوجد خالداً، وأوجد خالد بكراً، وأوجد بكر زيداً الذي هو أول السلسلة، فزيد متوقف على بكر الذي هو متوقف على زيد بواسطتين. وأن عمراً أحدث زيداً، فقد توقف زيد على عمرو المتوقف عليه، وإن لم تتناه المُحْدَثون؛ لَزِمَ التسلسلُ : وهو تتابعُ الأشياء واحداً بعد واحد إلى ما لا نهاية له في الزمن الماضي، كما لو فُرِضَ أن زيداً أحدثه عمرو، وأن عمراً أحدثه بكر، وأن بكراً أحدثه خالد، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له، فقد تتابَعَتِ المُحْدَثون واحداً بعد واحد إلى ما لا نهاية له في الزمن الماضي.
(His saying: "As for the proof of the necessity of pre-eternity for Him, exalted, it is because... etc.") This proof is only completed by three syllogisms, and their arrangement is thus: if He were not pre-eternal, then He would be temporal, but His being temporal is impossible; for if He were temporal, He would need a creator, but His need for a creator is impossible; for if He needed a creator, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, and both are impossible. The easier way to arrange the implications is to say: if He were not pre-eternal, then He would be temporal; if He were temporal, then He would need a creator; if He needed a creator, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, and both are impossible. Thus, that which leads to them—i.e., His need for a creator—is impossible; so that which leads to it—i.e., His being temporal—is impossible; so that which leads to it—i.e., His not being pre-eternal—is impossible; thus its opposite is established, and that is what is sought. The author's method in the main text approximates this, as he limited the arrangement of implications to the way he mentioned. So reflect. (His saying: "If He were not pre-eternal, then He would be temporal") The reason for the inseparability between the antecedent and the consequent is that existence is confined to the pre-eternal and the temporal; so when something is not pre-eternal, it is temporal. (His saying: "So He would need a creator") i.e., because it is not valid that it occurred by itself, otherwise it would entail that one of two equal things be equal to its counterpart and yet preponderant over it without cause, which is impossible due to the combination of equality and preponderance as previously mentioned. (His saying: "So there would follow circularity or infinite regress") i.e., because if it needs a creator, then its creator would also need a creator due to the establishment of likeness between them. Then if the creators terminate, circularity follows: it is the dependence of a thing on something upon which it depends, as if one supposes that Zayd brought about 'Amr, and 'Amr brought about Zayd. (Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: "Because He" etc.) i.e., it is said in its explanation: pre-eternity is necessary for Him because... And if he said: it is that if it were not... etc., making both pronouns refer back to the proof, it would be clearer, and likewise is said in all that follows because the intent is the explanation of the proof itself. (His saying: "As if one supposes that Zayd... etc.") And the like of that is if Zayd brought about 'Amr, and Khalid, and Khalid brought about Bakr, and Bakr brought about Zayd who is the first of the chain, then Zayd depends on Bakr who depends on Zayd through two intermediaries. And that 'Amr brought about Zayd, so Zayd depends on 'Amr upon whom he depends; and if the creators do not terminate, infinite regress follows: it is the succession of things one after another without end in past time, as if one supposes that Zayd was brought about by 'Amr, and 'Amr by Bakr, and Bakr by Khalid, and so on without end, so the creators have succeeded one after another without end in past time.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the proof for God's pre-eternity (qidam). The commentator explains that the proof requires three syllogisms and provides two arrangements: one presented in the main text (by Sanusi) and an easier one. The core argument is: if God were not pre-eternal, He would be temporal; if temporal, He would need a creator; if He needed a creator, that creator would also need a creator, leading either to circularity (if the chain terminates) or infinite regress (if it does not terminate). Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible, so the premises that lead to them must be false, thereby affirming that God is pre-eternal. The commentator then explains the logical inseparability of the antecedent and consequent: existence is divided into only two categories—pre-eternal and temporal—so if something is not pre-eternal, it must be temporal. The need for a creator is justified by the fact that a thing cannot originate itself, otherwise that would imply that one of two equal things becomes preponderant over the other without cause, which is impossible. The impossibility of circularity and infinite regress is illustrated with examples involving hypothetical chains of creators (Zayd, 'Amr, etc.). The marginal note by al-Ajhuri comments on the phrasing of the proof and emphasizes clarity in the pronouns.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
محدث
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the proof is circular, but it is not.
Correction: The proof is a chain of implications starting from a premise accepted by the opponent (existence is limited to pre-eternal and temporal) and deducing an impossibility, which then forces the rejection of the original premise. It does not assume what it seeks to prove.

Checkpoint
What are the two impossible consequences that would follow from God needing a creator?
A) Circularity and infinite regress
B) Temporality and contingency
C) Pre-eternity and necessity
D) Causality and dependence

Key Takeaway
The proof for God's pre-eternity is based on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, which would follow if God were not pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now have a rational proof that only God is pre-eternal—everything else is temporal and dependent. What does that mean for the things you rely on daily for security and happiness? Are you treating any created thing as if it were eternal? What would it look like to truly depend on God alone?
?
The proof shows that circularity and infinite regress are impossible in reality. Consider your own thoughts: Is there any area of your life where you are stuck in a 'cycle' of worry, sin, or worldly pursuits that never ends? How might acknowledging God as the beginning and end break that cycle?
?
The proof's conclusion is that God must be pre-eternal. If you truly believed that God is the only eternal reality, how would your priorities change today? What temporal distractions would lose their power over you? Ask Allah to make this truth settle in your heart.

The Universal Principle and the Objection of Pre-Eternal Non-Existence
Al-Bajuri presents the universal principle, objection, and two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal principle that necessarily eternal things cannot cease, the objection raised against it from pre-eternal non-existence, and the two answers given to defend the principle.

Core Idea
Necessary eternity entails impossibility of ceasing — but only for existent things.

Al-Bajuri presents a universal principle derived from the necessary eternity of Allah: anything truly eternal cannot later become non-existent. This seems straightforward, but an objection arises from our own pre-eternal non-existence. The passage walks through the objection and two responses, asking you to reflect on the subtlety of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then follow the literal translation. Notice the structure: principle, objection, first answer, second answer, and the author's reflective comment.
The author ends with 'ta'ammal', signaling that careful thought is needed.
2
Identify the universal principle
The principle states: anyone whose eternity is necessary, its non-existence is impossible. Understand why this is considered the only agreed-upon doctrinal issue among rational people.
This principle is derived from Allah's necessary eternity.
3
Understand the objection
The objection points to our pre-eternal non-existence. It seems to fit the principle: it is necessarily eternal (we never existed in pre-eternity), yet it does cease with our creation.
The objection challenges the universal applicability of the principle.
4
Examine the two answers
The first answer restricts the principle to existent things. The second answer tries to deny the objection entirely, but the author notes a flaw: it only shows impossibility in pre-eternity, not absolute impossibility.
Compare the strength of each answer.
5
Reflect on the author's concluding note
The author says 'ta'ammal' — reflect. He leaves the issue open, suggesting that the objection has some force. Consider how this deepens your understanding of kalam methodology.
This reflects the scholarly practice of presenting arguments honestly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَريباً وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى يُؤخَذُ من ذلك أن كل من وَجَبَ قِدَمُه استحال عدمه، ولم تتَّفِقِ العُقَلاءُ على مسألة اعتقادية إلهية إلا هذه القاعدة الكلية. وأُورِدَ عليها: عَدَمُنا الأزلي، فإنه وَجَبَ قِدَمُه ولم يَسْتَحِلْ عدمه. وأجيب: بأن القاعدة مفروضة في الوجودي. وبعضهم منع الإيراد من أصله: بأن عَدَمَنا الأزلي يستحيلُ عَدَمُه؛ لأنه لو عُدمَ لوُجِدْنا في الأزل، ووجودنا في الأزل محال؛ لأنه لا يُوجَدُ فيه إلا الله وصفاته، وفيه: أنه إنما يستحيل عَدَمُه في الأزل لما ذكر، وهذا لا ينافي أنه ينعدم بتناهي الأزل، فيصدق عليه أنه وَجَبَ قِدَمُه ولم يَسْتَحِلْ عدمه. فتأمل.
His statement: 'And it has recently preceded that His eternity is necessary (wujub qidamihi ta'ala). It is taken from that that everyone whose eternity is necessary, its non-existence is impossible (istihala 'adamuhu). And the rational people have agreed upon no doctrinal divine issue except this universal principle. An objection was raised against it: Our pre-eternal non-existence ('adamuna al-azali) – its eternity is necessary but its non-existence is not impossible. And it was answered: The principle is posited concerning existent things (wujudi). And some prevented the objection from its root: that our pre-eternal non-existence, its non-existence is impossible; because if it were non-existent, we would exist in pre-eternity, and our existence in pre-eternity is impossible because nothing exists in it except Allah and His attributes. But it is said: Its non-existence is only impossible in pre-eternity due to what was mentioned, and this does not contradict that it becomes non-existent at the end of pre-eternity, so it is true of it that its eternity is necessary and its non-existence is not impossible. So reflect.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a universal principle derived from the necessary eternity of Allah: anything that is necessarily eternal cannot cease to exist. This is said to be the only doctrinal issue agreed upon by all rational people. Then an objection is raised: our pre-eternal non-existence (i.e., the fact that we did not exist in pre-eternity) is itself necessarily eternal (it has always been non-existent), yet it is not impossible for it to cease to exist (since we come into existence, removing that non-existence). The first answer is that the principle applies only to existent things, not to non-existence. Another answer claims that our pre-eternal non-existence cannot cease to exist because if it ceased, we would have existed in pre-eternity, which is impossible. But the author notes that this only shows it is impossible in pre-eternity, not that it cannot cease at the end of pre-eternity (i.e., when we are created, the pre-eternal non-existence ends). So the original objection stands partially. The comment ends with 'ta'ammal' (reflect), indicating the issue is subtle.

Key Terms

استحال العدم
العدم الأزلي
الوجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universal principle that necessarily eternal things cannot cease applies to non-existents like pre-eternal non-existence.
Correction: The principle is restricted to existent things, as the first answer clarifies. Non-existence is not subject to the same rule.

Checkpoint
Why is the objection about our pre-eternal non-existence not fully refuted by the second answer?
A) Because the second answer only shows impossibility in pre-eternity, not absolute impossibility
B) Because the objection is invalid
C) Because the principle applies to non-existence
D) Because the second answer denies the objection entirely

Key Takeaway
The universal principle that necessarily eternal things cannot cease only applies to existents, and even then careful reasoning is needed when objections arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire existence began from nothingness just moments ago in the scale of eternity, how does that reshape your sense of self-importance? Are you living with the humility that comes from knowing you are not eternal?
?
The passage shows that only what is necessarily eternal cannot cease. You are not eternal. Are you living as if your time here is limited and precious, or do you act as if you will never face your own non-existence?
?
Al-Bajuri ends with 'ta'ammal' (reflect). Reflect now: The very fact that you exist after being non-existent is a sign that your existence is a gift from the Eternal. How can you let that awareness change your gratitude and obedience today?

The Proof from Need for a Specifier
Al-Bajuri comments on the proof from need for a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why needing a specifier entails that something is originated, and why this cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Only originated things need a cause; Allah is pre-eternal and needs none.

In this brief commentary, al-Bajuri explains a key logical argument from the matn: if Allah needed a specifier (mukhasis), He would be originated (hadith). This is because need for a cause is a property of contingent, originated beings, not the Pre-eternal. Let's examine the precise reasoning and its implication for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation.
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of the commentary.
2
Identify the conditional statement and conclusion.
The passage presents a conditional: 'if He needed a specifier, He would be originated.' Identify the 'if' part and the 'then' part.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the entailment.
Al-Bajuri explains why: only the originated (hadith) needs a specifier; the pre-eternal (qadim) does not. Ask yourself why that is the case.
This reasoning is based on the principle that whatever is pre-eternal is self-sufficient.
4
Reflect on the implication for Allah.
Since Allah is described as the Pre-eternal (Qadim), He cannot need a specifier. This argument reinforces His absolute independence.
This is a classic argument for Allah's eternity and self-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَلَوِ احْتَاجَ إِلَى مُخَصّص) أي: مُوجِدٍ. (وقوله: لَكَانَ حَادِثاً أي لأنه لا يحتاجُ لذلك إلا الحادث؛ إذ القديم لا يحتاج له كما لا يخفى.
His saying: 'and if He needed a specifier (mukhasis)' i.e., an originator. And His saying: 'He would be originated' i.e., because only the originated needs that; for the pre-eternal does not need it, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof refuting the need for a specifier for Allah. Al-Bajuri comments on the author's statement: if Allah were to need a specifier (mukhasis), that would entail that He is originated (hadith), because only originated things require a cause or specifier. The pre-eternal (qadim) does not need anything external for its existence. This is a straightforward argument from the impossibility of an eternal thing needing a cause.

Key Terms

حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that even the Eternal could need a specifier.
Correction: The passage clarifies that need for a specifier is exclusive to originated things; the Pre-eternal (qadim) has no need.

Checkpoint
Why does needing a specifier entail that something is originated?
A) Because the specifier is also originated.
B) Because only originated things are in need of a cause for their existence.
C) Because the specifier is pre-eternal.
D) Because the specifier is an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Need for a specifier is a sign of origination; therefore, Allah, being Pre-eternal, cannot need any specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
What would it mean for you today to act as if Allah is utterly self-sufficient? What comfort or fear does that bring?
