The Conditional Proof of Divine Oneness
Al-Bajuri presents the conditional proof of oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic logical structure of the proof for God's oneness: if God were not one, nothing would exist; but the world does exist, so God must be one.

Core Idea
If God were not one, nothing would exist – but the world exists, so God is one.

Before diving into complex details, Al-Bajuri presents a crisp conditional argument that establishes God's oneness. This argument relies on a simple logical form and appeals to observable reality. Understanding this core reasoning is essential before exploring the nuanced scenarios that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement, an observation, and a conclusion.
The translation is literal; focus on the structure.
2
Identify the conditional and the exception
Al-Bajuri states the conditional (if not one, then no world) and mentions that the exception premise (but world exists) is omitted because it is obvious. Understand why it is obvious.
The omitted premise is the observation that the world exists.
3
Reflect on the summary nature
The passage notes that this is a summary formulation, not addressing details about quantity in essence, attributes, or actions. This sets the stage for the detailed proof in the next lesson.
These details will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الوَحْدَانِيَّة لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلانه ... إلخ) تقرير هذا البرهان :هكذا لو لم يكن واحداً للزم أن لا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ من العالم، لكن عَدَمُ وجود شيء من العالم باطل بالمشاهَدَةِ، فبطَلَ ما أدى إليه، وهو عَدَمُ كونه واحداً، وإذا بَطَلَ ذلك؛ ثبت نقيضُه، وهو المطلوب. فالمصنف ذَكَرَ الشرطية، وحذف الاستثنائية لظهورها. وهذا تقرير على سبيل الإجمال؛ لعدم التعرض فيه لنفي الكم المنفصل في الذات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الصفات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الأفعال والمتصل فيها على ما مرّ. وبيانُ الأول: أنه لو كان هناك إلهان مثلاً لأمكن اختلافهما بأن يريد أحدهما وجود شيء والآخرُ ،عدمه، وحينئذ يلزم عَجْزُهما؛ لأنه لا يُمْكِنُ أَن يَنْفُذَ مرادهما معاً؛ لأنه يلزم عليه اجتماع النقيضين، ولا مُرَادُ أحدهما دون الآخر؛ لأنه يلزم عَجْزُ الذي لم يَنْفُذُ مرادُهُ، والآخر مثله، فيلزم عجزه أيضاً. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَالْآخَرُ مِثْلُهُ... إلخ) إن أريد بلزوم عجزه: إثبات عجزه عن شيء آخر غير ما نفذت به إرادته لم يصح؛ لأن المقصود: إثبات العجز المؤدي إلى عدم وجود شيء من الحوادث، والعجز على هذا الوجه قد وجد معه بعض الحوادث، وهو ما نفذت به الإرادة وإن أريد به العجز بالكلية حتى عما نفذت به الإرادة فهذا لا يقول به عاقل؛ لأن الفرض واقع، فالوجه الوجيه أن يقال على تقدير اختلافهما لا جائز أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين وهما الوجود والعدم، ولا أن ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث. والظاهر أن هذا مرادهم، لكن عبارتهم بعيدة عن إفادته. ثم إن المحشي تكلم على تقدير اختلافهما وسكت على تقدير اتفاقهما على وجود شيء، وبيانه أنه لا جائز حينئذ أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد؛ لأن المسألة مفروضة في الجوهر الفرد، ولا ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث.
His saying: 'As for the proof of the necessity of oneness (wahdaniyya) for Him, the Exalted, it is because... etc.' The formulation of this proof is thus: If He were not one, it would necessarily follow that nothing of the world would exist, but the non-existence of anything of the world is false by observation, so what led to it is false, which is His not being one, and when that is false, its opposite is established, which is the required. So the author mentioned the conditional premise and omitted the exceptional premise because it is obvious. And this is a formulation in summary form, because it does not address the negation of discrete quantity in essence and continuous quantity therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in attributes and continuous therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in actions and continuous therein, as previously mentioned. And the explanation of the first: if there were, for example, two gods, their disagreement would be possible, such that one wills the existence of something and the other its non-existence, and then their impotence follows because it is not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out together because it would entail the conjunction of two contradictories, nor can the intention of one alone be carried out to the exclusion of the other, because that would entail the impotence of the one whose intention is not carried out, and the other is like him, so his impotence also follows. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'and the other is like him... etc.' If by 'his impotence follows' is meant establishing his impotence regarding something other than that which his will carried out, it is not valid, because the intended is to establish impotence that leads to the non-existence of any of the events, and impotence of this sort has some events existing alongside it, namely that which the will carried out. And if by it is meant total impotence, even regarding that which the will carried out, then no rational person would say that, because the hypothetical is actual. So the correct approach is to say: on the supposition of their disagreement, it is not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out because of the conjunction of two contradictories—existence and non-existence—nor can the intention of one be carried out to the exclusion of the other because of the similarity between them, so their impotence is determined, and then nothing of the events would exist. And it appears that this is their intent, but their expression is far from conveying it. Then the commentator (al-Muhashshi) spoke on the supposition of their disagreement and remained silent on the supposition of their agreement on the existence of something. Its explanation is that it is then not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out because of the conjunction of two efficient causes on a single effect, because the issue is predicated on the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), nor can the intention of one be carried out to the exclusion of the other because of the similarity between them, so their impotence is determined, and then nothing of the events would exist.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of God's oneness (wahdaniyya). Al-Bajuri presents a conditional argument: if God were not one, nothing of the world would exist. But the world does exist (by observation), so the antecedent is false, meaning God is one. This is a concise formulation omitting the straightforward 'exception' premise. He then explains the detailed argument: suppose there were two gods. They could disagree, one willing a thing's existence and the other its non-existence. Then both cannot be fulfilled together (contradiction), nor can only one be fulfilled because the other would be impotent, and both are equal, so both would be impotent, leading to nothing existing. Al-Ajhuri's marginal note refines this: the impotence argued must be total—not just partial—otherwise existence could occur. He also addresses the case of agreement: if both agree, then having two causes on one effect (the indivisible atom) is impossible, so again they must be impotent. Thus, the only consistent conclusion is that there cannot be two gods.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الكم المنفصل
الكم المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only works if we accept the conditional without evidence.
Correction: The conditional is supported by the detailed argument in the next lesson, and the exception premise is based on observable reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the omitted premise in Al-Bajuri's proof?
A) God is one by nature.
B) The world exists by observation.
C) Two gods cannot agree.
D) Nothing can cause itself.

Key Takeaway
The world's existence directly proves God's oneness via a simple conditional argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's existence is the direct proof of God's oneness, then every moment you witness the world, you are reminded of that truth. When was the last time you paused to let the existence of a single thing—like a tree or a breath—drive you to affirm 'Lā ilāha illallāh' with full certainty? What will you do today to turn your observation into worship?
?
The proof shows that if God were not one, nothing would exist. Think of a time you felt your own existence was fragile or threatened. Did that experience lead you to a deeper trust in the One who alone sustains? How can you intentionally cultivate the awareness that your existence hangs on His oneness in your daily routines?
?
Al-Bajuri notes that this proof is a summary and does not cover every nuance. Many Muslims settle for a surface-level belief in oneness without deeper reflection. What is one aspect of tawḥīd—such as oneness in essence, attributes, or actions—that you have not fully contemplated? Commit to studying that aspect this week.

The Impossibility of Two Gods in Detail
Al-Bajuri details the impossibility of two gods via disagreement and agreement scenarios, with a gloss by Al-Ajhuri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two gods is impossible, whether they disagree or agree, by analyzing the logical consequences of each scenario.

Core Idea
Two gods would lead to impotence whether they disagree or agree.

After the summary proof, Al-Bajuri now explains the detailed reasoning behind the impossibility of two gods. He examines two cases: disagreement and agreement. In both cases, logical contradictions arise, leading to the conclusion that no world could exist if there were more than one god. A marginal note by Al-Ajhuri clarifies the precise meaning of 'impotence' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and translation. Pay attention to the two cases: disagreement and agreement of two hypothetical gods.
The passage includes a gloss by Al-Ajhuri that refines the argument.
2
Analyze the disagreement case
If two gods disagree, one willing existence and the other non-existence, neither total fulfillment nor partial fulfillment is possible. Understand why both lead to impotence.
Partial fulfillment would imply the other god is impotent, which contradicts their supposed divinity.
3
Analyze the agreement case
If both agree to create something, two efficient causes on one effect (the indivisible atom) is impossible, so again both must be impotent.
This is based on the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).
4
Synthesize the conclusion
In both scenarios, the existence of two gods leads to the conclusion that nothing of the world would exist. Since the world does exist, two gods cannot exist.
This supports the earlier conditional proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الوَحْدَانِيَّة لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلانه ... إلخ) تقرير هذا البرهان :هكذا لو لم يكن واحداً للزم أن لا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ من العالم، لكن عَدَمُ وجود شيء من العالم باطل بالمشاهَدَةِ، فبطَلَ ما أدى إليه، وهو عَدَمُ كونه واحداً، وإذا بَطَلَ ذلك؛ ثبت نقيضُه، وهو المطلوب. فالمصنف ذَكَرَ الشرطية، وحذف الاستثنائية لظهورها. وهذا تقرير على سبيل الإجمال؛ لعدم التعرض فيه لنفي الكم المنفصل في الذات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الصفات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الأفعال والمتصل فيها على ما مرّ. وبيانُ الأول: أنه لو كان هناك إلهان مثلاً لأمكن اختلافهما بأن يريد أحدهما وجود شيء والآخرُ ،عدمه، وحينئذ يلزم عَجْزُهما؛ لأنه لا يُمْكِنُ أَن يَنْفُذَ مرادهما معاً؛ لأنه يلزم عليه اجتماع النقيضين، ولا مُرَادُ أحدهما دون الآخر؛ لأنه يلزم عَجْزُ الذي لم يَنْفُذُ مرادُهُ، والآخر مثله، فيلزم عجزه أيضاً. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَالْآخَرُ مِثْلُهُ... إلخ) إن أريد بلزوم عجزه: إثبات عجزه عن شيء آخر غير ما نفذت به إرادته لم يصح؛ لأن المقصود: إثبات العجز المؤدي إلى عدم وجود شيء من الحوادث، والعجز على هذا الوجه قد وجد معه بعض الحوادث، وهو ما نفذت به الإرادة وإن أريد به العجز بالكلية حتى عما نفذت به الإرادة فهذا لا يقول به عاقل؛ لأن الفرض واقع، فالوجه الوجيه أن يقال على تقدير اختلافهما لا جائز أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين وهما الوجود والعدم، ولا أن ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث. والظاهر أن هذا مرادهم، لكن عبارتهم بعيدة عن إفادته. ثم إن المحشي تكلم على تقدير اختلافهما وسكت على تقدير اتفاقهما على وجود شيء، وبيانه أنه لا جائز حينئذ أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد؛ لأن المسألة مفروضة في الجوهر الفرد، ولا ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث.
His saying: 'As for the proof of the necessity of oneness (wahdaniyya) for Him, the Exalted, it is because... etc.' The formulation of this proof is thus: If He were not one, it would necessarily follow that nothing of the world would exist, but the non-existence of anything of the world is false by observation, so what led to it is false, which is His not being one, and when that is false, its opposite is established, which is the required. So the author mentioned the conditional premise and omitted the exceptional premise because it is obvious. And this is a formulation in summary form, because it does not address the negation of discrete quantity in essence and continuous quantity therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in attributes and continuous therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in actions and continuous therein, as previously mentioned. And the explanation of the first: if there were, for example, two gods, their disagreement would be possible, such that one wills the existence of something and the other its non-existence, and then their impotence follows because it is not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out together because it would entail the conjunction of two contradictories, nor can the intention of one alone be carried out to the exclusion of the other, because that would entail the impotence of the one whose intention is not carried out, and the other is like him, so his impotence also follows. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'and the other is like him... etc.' If by 'his impotence follows' is meant establishing his impotence regarding something other than that which his will carried out, it is not valid, because the intended is to establish impotence that leads to the non-existence of any of the events, and impotence of this sort has some events existing alongside it, namely that which the will carried out. And if by it is meant total impotence, even regarding that which the will carried out, then no rational person would say that, because the hypothetical is actual. So the correct approach is to say: on the supposition of their disagreement, it is not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out because of the conjunction of two contradictories—existence and non-existence—nor can the intention of one be carried out to the exclusion of the other because of the similarity between them, so their impotence is determined, and then nothing of the events would exist. And it appears that this is their intent, but their expression is far from conveying it. Then the commentator (al-Muhashshi) spoke on the supposition of their disagreement and remained silent on the supposition of their agreement on the existence of something. Its explanation is that it is then not possible for both of their intentions to be carried out because of the conjunction of two efficient causes on a single effect, because the issue is predicated on the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), nor can the intention of one be carried out to the exclusion of the other because of the similarity between them, so their impotence is determined, and then nothing of the events would exist.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of God's oneness (wahdaniyya). Al-Bajuri presents a conditional argument: if God were not one, nothing of the world would exist. But the world does exist (by observation), so the antecedent is false, meaning God is one. This is a concise formulation omitting the straightforward 'exception' premise. He then explains the detailed argument: suppose there were two gods. They could disagree, one willing a thing's existence and the other its non-existence. Then both cannot be fulfilled together (contradiction), nor can only one be fulfilled because the other would be impotent, and both are equal, so both would be impotent, leading to nothing existing. Al-Ajhuri's marginal note refines this: the impotence argued must be total—not just partial—otherwise existence could occur. He also addresses the case of agreement: if both agree, then having two causes on one effect (the indivisible atom) is impossible, so again they must be impotent. Thus, the only consistent conclusion is that there cannot be two gods.

Key Terms
النقيضين
المماثلة
الجوهر الفرد
الكم المنفصل
الكم المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could exist if they always agree and cooperate.
Correction: Even if they agree, having two causes for one effect is impossible, as per the indivisible atom principle, so they would be impotent.

Checkpoint
What is the principle used in the agreement scenario to show impossibility?
A) The conjunction of contradictories.
B) The indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).
C) The similarity of the gods.
D) The observation of the world.

Key Takeaway
Both agreement and disagreement among two gods lead to their impotence and the non-existence of the world, proving that only one God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If two gods could exist, your life would be subject to conflicting wills, leaving you in constant confusion. Knowing that only Allah has absolute power, does your heart truly entrust all affairs to Him alone, or do you still seek control from other sources? What specific fear or habit reveals that you haven't fully surrendered?
?
The passage shows that two gods would lead to impotence and the impossibility of anything existing. Reflect on a time when you felt your own efforts were futile. Did that feeling drive you to rely solely on Allah, or did it lead to despair? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His sole sovereignty over your life?
?
Allah's absolute uniqueness is not a mere theological fact; it demands that He alone be your object of love, fear, and hope. Is there a relationship, ambition, or possession that competes with your devotion to Him? What concrete step will you take today to dethrone that rival and reaffirm 'La ilaha illa Allah' in your heart?

The Conditional Proof of Divine Oneness
Sanusi presents the proof of oneness via conditional syllogism and the conflict scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves divine oneness using a conditional syllogism and why two gods cannot coexist.

Core Idea
If two gods could disagree, nothing could exist — so God must be one.

Sanusi begins by outlining the overall proof of God's oneness in the form of a conditional syllogism. The argument is simple yet powerful: if there were more than one god, their conflicting wills would prevent anything from existing. But the world does exist, so there must be one God. In this passage, he first presents the condensed proof and then elaborates on the impossibility of two gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Read the Arabic passage and identify the conditional premise ('if not one, then nothing exists') and the conclusion. Notice that Sanusi omits the explicit divisive syllogism because it is obvious.
The text says the author 'mentioned the conditional premise and omitted the divisive.'
2
Understand the two-gods scenario
The passage then explains what would happen if two gods existed. Imagine they have different wills. Why can't both wills be fulfilled? Why can't only one be fulfilled? Work through the logic.
The key is the impossibility of contradictories (nāqiḍayn).
3
Connect to the overall oneness proof
Now see how the two-gods scenario supports the initial conditional. If even one scenario leads to impossibility, then the original conditional holds. This strengthens the proof.
The proof is not just logical; it is grounded in observed existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وأما برهان وجوب الوحدانية له تعالى فلاعه [؟] ... إلخ) تقرير هذا البرهان :هكذا لو لم يكن واحداً للزم أن لا يوجد شيءٌ من العالم، لكن عدم وجود شيء من العالم باطل بالمشاهدة، فبطل ما أدى إليه، وهو عدم كونه واحداً، وإذا بطل ذلك؛ ثبت نقيضه، وهو المطلوب. فالمصنف ذكر الشرطية، وحذف الاستثنائية لظهورها. وهذا تقرير على سبيل الإجمال؛ لعدم التعرض فيه لنفي الكم المنفصل في الذات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الصفات والمتصل فيها، ولنفي الكم المنفصل في الأفعال والمتصل فيها على ما مرّ. وبيان الأول: أنه لو كان هناك إلهان مثلاً لأمكن اختلافهما بأن يريد أحدهما وجود شيء والآخر عدمه، وحينئذ يلزم عجزهما؛ لأنه لا يمكن أن ينفذ مرادهما معاً؛ لأنه يلزم عليه اجتماع النقيضين، ولا مراد أحدهما دون الآخر؛ لأنه يلزم عجز الذي لم ينفذ مراده، والآخر مثله، فيلزم عجزه أيضاً.
His statement: 'As for the proof of the necessity of oneness for Him, may He be exalted, ... etc.' The exposition of this proof is as follows: If He were not one, it would necessitate that nothing of the world exist, but the non-existence of anything from the world is false by observation, so what leads to it is false, namely His not being one. And if that is false, its opposite is established, which is what is desired. So the author mentioned the conditional premise and omitted the divisive [syllogism] due to its obviousness. And this is an exposition in summary, because it does not address the negation of discrete quantity in essence and continuous quantity therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in attributes and continuous therein, nor the negation of discrete quantity in actions and continuous therein, as has preceded. And the explanation of the first [point]: If there were, for example, two gods, their differing would be possible, such that one wills the existence of something and the other its non-existence. Then their inability would be necessary, because it is impossible that both of their wills be executed together, because that would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictories, nor [can] the will of one of them be executed without the other, because that would necessitate the inability of the one whose will is not executed, and the other is like him, so his inability also follows.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of God's oneness. It begins by stating the proof in the form of a conditional syllogism: if God were not one, then nothing would exist; but something does exist (by observation), so the antecedent is false and its opposite is true — God is one. The author then notes that this is a summary presentation, as it does not cover all aspects of oneness (negation of quantity in essence, attributes, and actions). He then elaborates on the first aspect, explaining why two gods cannot coexist: if they differed in will, neither will could be fulfilled because simultaneous fulfillment entails contradiction, and fulfilling only one will implies the inability of the other. Thus both would be unable, leading to the non-existence of all things. This establishes the necessity of oneness.

Key Terms
برهان
وحدانية
شرطية
استثنائية
نقيضين
كم منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is merely a logical exercise without empirical basis.
Correction: The proof hinges on the observed existence of the world (المشاهدة) and the real impossibility of simultaneous contradictory wills.

Checkpoint
What form of syllogism is used in the proof of divine oneness as described?
A) A categorical syllogism
B) A conditional syllogism with a hidden divisive premise
C) A disjunctive syllogism
D) An inductive argument

Key Takeaway
The proof of divine oneness relies on a conditional syllogism: if there were more than one God, existence would be impossible; but the world exists, so there must be one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that if two gods could disagree, nothing would exist. In your life, where are you torn between two contradictory desires or loyalties? Is there some area where your inner conflict has produced a kind of 'non-existence'—a failure to act, a paralysis, or a lack of peace? How does the oneness of God call you to resolve that conflict?
?
You observe that the world exists. But do you live as if that existence points to a single, undivided source? Where do you act as if your own life depends on multiple, conflicting 'gods'—like money, reputation, or comfort—and thereby deny the very proof you've just read? What would change if you took the conditional syllogism as literally true for your decisions today?
?
The author says the divisive syllogism is 'obvious' and omitted. Is God's oneness so obvious in your daily experience? Or have you become blind to it? What would it take for you to see the world as the proof describes it—a direct testimony to the impossibility of any rival to God?

Clarifying the Necessity of Total Inability
Al-Ajhuri distinguishes between partial and total inability to fix the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ajhuri insists that the inability of the two gods must be total, not partial, for the proof of divine oneness to hold.

Core Idea
Partial inability fails to prove oneness; total inability is necessary.

Before affirming divine oneness, we must ensure the argument for impossibility is airtight. Al-Ajhuri addresses a key ambiguity: does the inability proven apply only to something else, or completely? This lesson unpacks his clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic text carefully. Notice how al-Ajhuri starts by critiquing an ambiguous phrasing.
He is addressing the words 'and the other is like him'.
2
Identify the two meanings of inability
Al-Ajhuri presents two interpretations of 'inability'. Find them and see why each is problematic.
3
Understand the proper conclusion
See how al-Ajhuri then gives the correct statement: when wills differ, both cannot execute or one alone, leading to total inability.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the necessity of total inability
Why does partial inability not suffice? Because it would still allow some events, contradicting the goal of proving no events from two gods.
Think about the ultimate purpose of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: والآخر مثله... إلخ) إن أريد بلزوم عجزه: إثبات عجزه عن شيء آخر غير ما نفذت به إرادته لم يصح؛ لأن المقصود: إثبات العجز المؤدي إلى عدم وجود شيء من الحوادث، والعجز على هذا الوجه قد وجد معه بعض الحوادث، وهو ما نفذت به الإرادة وإن أريد به العجز بالكلية حتى عما نفذت به الإرادة فهذا لا يقول به عاقل؛ لأن الفرض واقع، فالوجه الوجيه أن يقال على تقدير اختلافهما لا جائز أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين وهما الوجود والعدم، ولا أن ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث. والظاهر أن هذا مرادهم، لكن عبارتهم بعيدة عن إفادته. ثم إن المحشي تكلم على تقدير اختلافهما وسكت على تقدير اتفاقهما على وجود شيء، وبيانه أنه لا جائز حينئذ أن ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد؛ لأن المسألة مفروضة في الجوهر الفرد، ولا ينفذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، فتعين عجزهما، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من الحوادث.
The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His statement: 'and the other is like him... etc.' If by the necessity of his inability is meant proving his inability regarding something other than what his will was executed upon, it is not valid because the intended [point] is proving the inability that leads to the non-existence of any of the events, and inability in this manner already exists alongside some events, namely what the will was executed upon. And if by it is meant total inability even regarding what the will was executed upon, then no sensible person would say that, because the [original] assumption is actual. So the proper way is to say: on the assumption of their differing, it is not possible that both wills be executed due to the conjunction of two contradictories, namely existence and non-existence, nor that the will of one be executed without the other due to the establishment of similarity between them. Thus their inability is determined, and then no event exists. And it appears that this is their intended meaning, but their expression is far from conveying it. Then the commentator [al-Bajuri] spoke on the assumption of their differing and remained silent on the assumption of their agreement on the existence of something. Its explanation: It is not possible then that both wills be executed because that would necessitate the conjunction of two efficient causes on one effect, since the matter is assumed to be about the individual atom. Nor can the will of one be executed without the other due to the establishment of similarity between them, so their inability is determined, and then no event exists.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Ajhuri that refines the argument for divine oneness. He addresses an ambiguity in the earlier formulation: when it is said that if one god's will is not executed, that god is unable, the critic might object that inability is only partial, not total. Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the intended inability must be total, leading to no events at all. He then extends the argument to the case where both gods agree on a single act. In that case, two independent agents acting on one indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) would require a conjunction of causes impossible by convention, so again both are unable. Thus the proof is complete for both the case of differing and of agreeing wills.

Key Terms
عجز
اجتماع النقيضين
مماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only shows that one of the two gods is unable regarding some events, not all.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the intended inability must be total, meaning neither god can execute any will, so no events occur.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with interpreting 'inability' as partial?
A) It is impossible by definition
B) It still allows some events, so the proof fails
C) It requires both gods to be equal
D) It contradicts the assumption

Key Takeaway
Al-Ajhuri shows that for the proof of oneness to be valid, the inability must be total, leaving no events at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If any imagined partner of Allah must be totally unable to bring about even a single event, what does that mean for your trust in Allah alone when you face a situation where your plans seem to conflict with someone else's? Are you ever tempted to attribute outcomes to other than Allah?
?
Al-Ajhuri had to clarify a subtle point because 'their expression is far from conveying it.' Has your own grasp of Tawheed ever been clouded by imprecise phrases or incomplete understandings? What specific step will you take this week to sharpen your conviction in the oneness of Allah?
?
The proof shows that two gods must be either both unable or one unable—but since neither can be executed alone, both are unable. How often do you mentally entertain the possibility that something else in the universe can act independently of Allah's will? What would it take to purge that thought entirely from your heart?

The Impossibility of Two Gods Acting in Agreement
Al-Ajhuri extends the argument to the case of agreed wills using the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even if two gods agree on a single act, their wills cannot both be executed, establishing their inability and thus divine oneness.

Core Idea
Agreement does not solve the problem; the atom's indivisibility prevents two causes.

Having dealt with differing wills, al-Ajhuri now addresses the scenario where the two gods agree. At first glance, agreement might seem possible, but he demonstrates that it leads to the impossibility of two causes in a single atom, again proving their inability.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part
Read from 'Then the commentator...' to the end. Notice that al-Ajhuri expands on what al-Bajuri omitted.
2
Understand the atom concept
The argument assumes the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Why is this crucial? Because two independent causes cannot act on one indivisible unit.
Without this assumption, the argument might not hold.
3
Examine the two impossibilities
First, both executing is impossible due to two causes on one effect. Second, one alone is impossible due to similarity. Thus both are unable.
4
Draw the overall conclusion
Thus even with agreement, no events exist from two gods, reinforcing the proof of oneness.
The argument is now complete for all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ibn Rushd's Objection and the Two Interpretations of Corruption
Sanusi presents Ibn Rushd's objection and discusses the meaning of corruption in Quran 21:22.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Rushd's objection to the conflict proof and how the interpretation of 'corruption' affects the logical status of the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
The Quranic proof for oneness can be persuasive or definitive depending on how you interpret 'corruption'.

This lesson examines a key objection raised by Ibn Rushd against the standard argument from conflict. It also explores the two main interpretations of the key term 'corruption' in Quran 21:22, which determines whether the verse provides a rational or a customary proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Ibn Rushd's objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice how Sanusi first states that the proof is common, then reports Ibn Rushd's objection.
Ibn Rushd's objection actually reinforces the proof.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'corruption'
Find the two meanings given for 'al-fasad' in the verse. The first is 'non-existence' (preponderant), the second is 'ruin/departure from order'.
This distinction is crucial for the type of proof.
3
Understand how the second interpretation affects the proof
If corruption means ruin due to customary conflict among multiple rulers, then the correlation is customary, not rational, making the verse a persuasive proof (hujja iqna'iyya) rather than a definitive one.
Persuasive proofs are still valid but not logically necessary.
4
Note the direction to further study
The matn says the explanation of the second interpretation is taken up by al-Saktani, but with criticisms, so consult his work.
This shows the tradition of scholarly debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي وهذا هو الدائر بين الجمهور. ويحكى عن ابن رشد أنه كان يقول: إذا قدر نفوذ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر؛ كان الذي نفذ مراده هو الإله، وتم دليل الوحدانية، وهذا الدليل هو المشار إليه بقوله تعالى: لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَنَا؛ لأن المراد بالفساد في الآية: عدم الوجود على الراجح. وقيل: المراد به الخراب والخروج عن هذا النظام؛ لما تقرر عادة من فساد المملكة عند تعدد الملوك، وعلى هذا تكون الملازمة التعدد والفساد عادية بين لا عقلية، وتكون الآية حجة إقناعية، بمعنى أنه يقنع بها الخصم، لا قطعية. وبيان كل من الثاني وما بعده قد تكفل به السكتاني وغيره، لكن فيه مناقشات ومؤاخذات. فانظره. (قوله: لو لم يكن واحدا أي في ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله كما علمته مما مر. (قوله: للزوم عجزه حينئذ أي: حين إذ لم يكن واحدا، وهذا تعليل لترتب انتفاء وجود شيء من العالم على عدم كونه واحداً، وقد تقدم توضيحه في الجملة.
The text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abdullah al-Sanusi: And this is the common [argument] among the majority. And it is narrated from Ibn Rushd that he used to say: If the execution of the will of one of them without the other is assumed, then the one whose will is executed is the God, and the proof of oneness is complete. And this proof is the one indicated by His statement, the Most High: 'If there were gods in them other than Allah, they would both have been corrupted' [Quran 21:22]. Because what is meant by corruption in the verse is non-existence, according to the preponderant view. And it is said: what is meant is ruin and departure from this order, due to what is conventionally established of the corruption of a kingdom when there are multiple kings. And on this [interpretation], the correlation between multiplicity and corruption is customary, not rational, and the verse becomes a persuasive proof, meaning that the opponent is persuaded by it, not a definitive proof. And the explanation of each of the second and what follows has been undertaken by al-Saktani and others, but therein are criticisms and objections. So consult it. His statement: 'If He were not one' means in His essence, attributes, and actions, as you have learned from what preceded. His statement: 'Due to the necessity of His inability then' means: at the time when He is not one, and this is a cause for the dependence of the non-existence of anything from the world on His not being one, and its clarification has preceded in summary.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the matn of Sanusi and glosses. The matn first states that the proof (of oneness by conflict) is common among scholars. It then reports Ibn Rushd's objection: if we assume one will prevails, then that one is the true God, and the proof still works. The matn then connects this to Quran 21:22, explaining that 'corruption' (fasad) means non-existence (the preponderant view) or ruin of the cosmic order. In the latter interpretation, the connection between multiplicity and corruption is customary, not rational, making the verse a persuasive argument rather than a definitive logical proof. The two glosses at the end clarify terms: 'if He were not one' refers to oneness in essence, attributes, and actions; 'due to the necessity of His inability' explains that inability results from not being one, which leads to non-existence.

Key Terms
ابن رشد
فساد
حجة إقناعية
عادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse is always a purely rational proof.
Correction: The verse can be interpreted as a rational proof (if corruption means non-existence) or a customary persuasive proof (if corruption means ruin).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preponderant interpretation of 'corruption' (al-fasad) in Quran 21:22?
A) Moral corruption
B) Destruction of the heavens
C) Non-existence of the world
D) Political chaos

Key Takeaway
Ibn Rushd's objection does not undermine the proof; the verse's interpretation can make it either a rational or a customary persuasive argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between a rational (aqli) proof and a customary (adi) persuasive proof. Which type has played a stronger role in your personal conviction of Allah's oneness? Are you satisfied with relying on customary evidence, or do you demand absolute logical certainty? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart?
?
Ibn Rushd's objection initially appears to challenge the proof, but it actually reinforces it. Consider a doubt you have faced about your faith—could it be that the doubt itself, if properly examined, leads to a deeper affirmation? Will you take time this week to probe one such doubt prayerfully rather than dismiss it?
?
The Quranic verse can be interpreted in two ways, each with different theological implications. In your own study of the Quran, do you settle for a single interpretation that feels comfortable, or do you actively seek out the range of scholarly views? How might humility toward differing interpretations strengthen your faith rather than weaken it?

The Glosses on 'One' and 'Inability'
Sanusi's glosses clarify the meaning of oneness and the logical consequence of its absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the clarifying glosses that explain what it means for Allah to be one and how not being one leads to inability and non-existence of the world.

Core Idea
Oneness is in essence, attributes, and actions; not being one necessitates inability.

The matn concludes with two glosses that unpack key terms. First, being 'one' is explained as oneness in all aspects. Second, the 'inability' that would result from not being one is tied to the non-existence of creation. These clarifications reinforce the logical necessity of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on 'if He were not one'
Read the Arabic carefully. The gloss explains that 'one' means 'one in His essence, attributes, and actions'.
This is a comprehensive oneness, not just numerical.
2
Understand the meaning of 'inability'
Read the second gloss: 'due to the necessity of His inability then' explains that not being one causes inability, which prevents the existence of anything in the world.
This shows the logical consequence of denying oneness.
3
Connect to the earlier proof
Recall from previous lessons that the proof of oneness relies on the impossibility of two gods acting. This gloss shows that if the proof were broken, inability would result, making creation impossible.

Source Text

?

Ibn Rushd's Proof for Divine Oneness
Ibn Rushd's argument for tawhid and its Quranic reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Rushd's reductio argument for tawhid and its basis in the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Assuming two gods leads to a logical contradiction, proving only one God exists.

This lesson presents a classical argument for divine oneness attributed to the philosopher Ibn Rushd. He uses a hypothetical scenario involving two gods to demonstrate that only one can truly be God, and connects this reasoning to the Quranic verse. By working through this argument, you will see how kalam engages with both reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that Ibn Rushd begins with a conditional statement: 'if the will of one of them prevails...'
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument assumes two gods, then shows that only one can be effective, which contradicts the hypothesis. Therefore, the hypothesis is false.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Ibn Rushd identifies this argument as the meaning of the verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.' Reflect on how the verse supports the reasoning.
The verse provides a revealed foundation for the rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُحكى عن ابن رُشْد أنه كان يقول : إذا قُدِرَ نفوذُ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر؛ كان الذي نَفَذَ مرادُهُ هو الإله، وتَمَّ دليل الوحدانية، وهذا الدليل هو المشار إليه بقوله تعالى: {لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا}؛ لأن المراد بالفساد في الآية: عَدَمُ الوجود على الراجح. وقيل: المراد به الخراب والخروجُ عن هذا النظام؛ لما تقرَّرَ عادةً من فَسَاد المملكة عند تعدد الملوك، وعلى هذا تكونُ الملازَمَةُ بين التعدد والفساد عاديَّةً لا عقلية، وتكون الآيةُ حُجَّةً إقناعيةً، بمعنى أنه يُقْنَعُ بها الخصم، لا قطعية. وبيانُ كلّ من الثاني وما بعده قد تكفَّلَ به السكتاني وغيرُه، لكن فيه مناقشات ومؤاخذات. فانظره. (قوله: هُوَ الدَّائِرُ بَيْنَ الْجُمْهُورِ) أي الجاري على ألسنتهم المشهور بينهم. (قوله: إِذَا قُدِرَ أشار به إلى أن هذا غير ممكن لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما، لكن بتقدير وجوده يكون الذي نفذ مراده هو الإله، وهذا هو المعقول. (وقوله: وَتَمَّ دَلِيلُ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةِ) معنى تمامه أنه إذا نفذ مراد أحدهما وثبت أنه الإله دون الآخر؛ أدى ذلك إلى بطلان المفروض، وهو تعدد الإله، ومتى بطل التعدد ثبتت الوحدانية، وهو المطلوب. (قوله: لَا قَطْعِيَّةً) لإمكان منع الملازمة.
It is narrated from Ibn Rushd that he used to say: If the execution of the will of one of them (two gods) is assumed without the other, then the one whose will was executed is the God, and the proof of oneness is complete. This proof is referred to in His saying, exalted: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.' Because the intended meaning of corruption in the verse, according to the preponderant view, is non-existence. And it is said: the intended meaning is ruin and departure from this order, due to what is customarily established of corruption of the kingdom when there are multiple kings. On this basis, the entailment between multiplicity and corruption is customary, not intellectual, and the verse becomes a persuasive proof, meaning that the opponent is convinced by it, not a cut-and-dried proof. The explanation of each of the second and what follows has been undertaken by al-Saktani and others, but there are discussions and objections in it. So look into it. (His saying: 'It is the one circulating among the majority') meaning it is current on their tongues, well-known among them. (His saying: 'If it is assumed') he indicated by this that this is impossible due to the similarity between them, but assuming its existence, the one whose will was executed is the God, and this is reasonable. (And his saying: 'And the proof of oneness is complete') its meaning of completeness is that if the will of one of them was executed and it is established that he is the God apart from the other, that leads to the nullification of the supposition, which is the multiplicity of gods; and whenever multiplicity is nullified, oneness is established, and that is the desired goal. (His saying: 'not cut-and-dried') because it is possible to deny the entailment.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for tawhid (divine oneness) attributed to Ibn Rushd and discusses the nature of its logical entailment. The proof begins with a hypothetical scenario: if two gods existed and one's will prevailed over the other's, then the one whose will prevailed would be the true God, thereby disproving the existence of two gods. This argument is linked to the Qur'anic verse (21:22) which states that if there were multiple gods, the heavens and earth would be corrupted. The author then discusses the interpretation of 'corruption' (fasād). The preponderant view is that it means non-existence of the world; another view is that it means disorder and ruin, based on the customary observation that multiple rulers lead to corruption. On this second view, the entailment (mulāzama) between multiplicity and corruption is customary (ˈāḍiyya), not strictly intellectual (ˈaqliyya). Consequently, the verse becomes a persuasive (iqnāʿiyya) rather than a cut-and-dried (qaṭʻiyya) proof. The author notes that the full explanation of this and subsequent points is found in al-Saktani's work, but there are discussions and criticisms. The marginal notes expand on key phrases: 'الدائر بين الجمهور' means it is common among scholars; 'إذا قدر' indicates the assumption is not possible due to the required similarity between two gods, but the argument is a reductio; 'وتم دليل الوحدانية' explains that the argument works by eliminating the hypothesis; and 'لا قطعية' notes that one could deny the customary entailment, so the proof is persuasive but not demonstrative.

Key Terms
توحيد
دليل
آلهة
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof is purely rational and independent of revelation.
Correction: Ibn Rushd explicitly links the proof to the Quranic verse, showing that reason and revelation agree on tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Rushd's argument, what happens if two gods exist and one's will prevails over the other's?
A) Both remain gods.
B) The one whose will prevails is the true God, the other is not.
C) The world is destroyed.
D) The two gods unite.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of multiple gods leads to a logical contradiction; only one God can truly exist, as reason and revelation confirm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as though there are multiple authorities in your life—trying to please God while also bowing to your own desires or society? How does Ibn Rushd's proof challenge you to submit to a single Sovereign?
?
When your will clashes with what you know God wills, do you truly believe that only His will ultimately prevails? How would your actions change if you lived with complete certainty in this truth?
?
If you internalized that the universe cannot sustain two gods, what does that imply about divided loyalties in your heart? Are you willing to uproot any 'god' competing for your ultimate allegiance today?

Customary vs Intellectual Entailment
Sanusi discusses the entailment in the verse and classifies the proof as persuasive or cut-and-dried based on the interpretation of corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the entailment in the proof can be considered customary and how that affects the strength of the argument.

Core Idea
The proof's strength depends on whether the connection between multiple gods and corruption is necessary (intellectual) or merely customary.

After presenting the proof, Sanusi discusses two interpretations of 'corruption' in the verse. One view treats the connection as necessary; the other sees it as based on customary observation. This distinction determines whether the proof is cut-and-dried (demonstrative) or merely persuasive. Understanding this nuance is key to appreciating how kalam evaluates arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of 'corruption'
Read the passage and note the two meanings of 'فساد' given: non-existence (the preponderant view) and ruin/disorder (another view).
The first view leads to a necessary entailment; the second to a customary one.
2
Understand the distinction between customary and intellectual entailment
An intellectual entailment is based on logical necessity; a customary one is based on regular pattern of events. The passage explains that on the second view, the entailment is customary.
3
See the consequence for the proof's strength
If the entailment is customary, the proof is persuasive (إقناعية) not cut-and-dried (قطعية), because the opponent could deny the customary connection.
The marginal note adds: 'it is possible to deny the entailment'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the proof from the verse is a definitive logical demonstration.
Correction: Actually, depending on the interpretation of 'corruption', the proof may be merely persuasive, based on customary analogy.

Checkpoint
According to the second interpretation of 'corruption' in the verse, what kind of entailment is between multiplicity of gods and corruption?
A) Intellectual entailment (عقلية)
B) Customary entailment (عادية)
C) Both intellectual and customary
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The strength of a proof can vary based on the interpretation of key terms; not every Quranic argument is demonstrative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a proof from the Quran, do you automatically assume it is logically airtight? Will you now examine the type of entailment behind it to avoid building your certainty on a customary assumption?
?
Are you willing to challenge yourself to distinguish between intellectual and customary reasoning in your own faith? Where in your beliefs might you be relying on customary arguments as if they were necessary?
?
If a person denies the customary analogy in this proof, could you still provide them with a demonstrative argument for tawhid? If not, what does that say about the foundation of your conviction?

Proof That God's Actions Are Rationally Permissible
Bajuri sets forth the proof that God's acts are neither necessary nor impossible, but permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's actions are neither rationally obligatory nor impossible, but permissible.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by rational necessity or impossibility.

This lesson presents a concise proof from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin, as explained by al-Bajuri. The argument uses a conditional syllogism to show that if God's actions were rationally necessary or impossible, the very nature of possibility would be contradicted. By understanding this proof, you will see how kalam establishes the freedom of divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the compact logical structure.
This passage is a proof, so pay attention to the 'if...then' structure.
2
Identify the conditional and exceptive parts
The proof has two parts: a conditional statement (if something were obligatory or impossible, then the possible would change) and an exceptive statement (that change is not rationally conceivable). Find both in the translation.
The exceptive is signaled by 'and that is not rationally conceivable'.
3
Understand why the consequent is false
Ask yourself: Can a possible thing become necessary or impossible by itself? The answer is no, because possibility is a fixed modal category. This falsity undermines the conditional, proving the antecedent (obligation or impossibility) is false.
This shows that God's acts are not bound by rational necessity or impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ كَوْنِ فِعْل الْمُمْكِنَاتِ أَوْ تَرْكِهَا جَائِزاً فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى تقريره أن تقول: لو وَجَبَ عليه شيءٌ منها عقلاً أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب الممكن واجباً أو مستحيلاً، لكن التالي باطل، فبَطَلَ المقدَّمُ. والمصنف ذَكَرَ الشرطية، وأشار إلى الاستثنائية بقوله: (وذلك لا يُعْقَلُ)؛ لأنه في قُوَّة أن يقول: لكن التالي محال.
His saying: 'As for the proof that the doing of possible things or their omission is permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He,' its exposition is that you say: If something of them were obligatory upon Him rationally, or impossible rationally, then the possible would become necessary or impossible; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. And the author mentioned the conditional and indicated the exceptive by his saying: 'And that is not rationally conceivable'; because it is as if he says: But the consequent is absurd.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God's actions are neither rationally necessary nor rationally impossible, but rather permissible (ja'iz). The proof is structured as a conditional syllogism: If something were rationally obligatory or impossible, then a possible thing would change into a necessary or impossible thing. But the consequent is false (since the possible cannot change its nature), so the antecedent is false—thus no rational obligation or impossibility attaches to God's actions. The commentator notes that the author (Sanusi) stated the conditional and pointed to the exceptive by saying 'and that is not rationally conceivable,' which functions as 'but the consequent is absurd.'

Key Terms
برهان
فعل الممكنات
جواز
وجوب
استحالة
انقلاب الممكن
شرطية
استثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that rational permissibility means God's actions are merely possible in the same way that created actions are possible.
Correction: Rational permissibility here means that God's actions are not subject to rational necessity or impossibility; they are freely chosen. The proof distinguishes modal judgments about God's acts, not the contingent nature of the acts themselves.

Checkpoint
What does the proof aim to establish?
A) That God's acts are necessary
B) That God's acts are rationally permissible
C) That God's acts are impossible
D) That God's acts are contingent in themselves

Key Takeaway
God's actions are rationally permissible, as shown by the impossibility of a possible thing becoming necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that God owes you something—health, wealth, or reward—based on your deeds? How does knowing that His actions are purely permissible, not necessary, challenge that assumption and call you to pure gratitude?
?
When you face a hardship that seems unfair, do you find yourself questioning God's wisdom or feeling entitled to an explanation? How would your heart shift if you truly accepted that God is never obligated by your standards of fairness?
?
How does this truth about God's freedom from rational necessity transform the way you pray? Are your prayers demands—or humble requests from a creature who knows the Creator owes nothing? Commit to one change in your prayer attitude starting today.

Divine Reward and the Principle of Non-Questionability
Sanusi presents an objection about reward without afflictions and answers with the principle of divine non-questionability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection regarding divine reward and the foundational kalam principle that Allah is not questioned about His actions.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are beyond human questioning.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection: could Allah not simply reward the prophets without subjecting them to trials? Sanusi presents this objection and then gives a concise but profound answer that anchors the divine attribute of absolute sovereignty. The answer is not a justification of means but a declaration of God's unique station above all interrogation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims that reward does not require afflictions because Allah can magnify rewards without them. Notice the logic: it is based on divine omnipotence.
2
Understand the answer
The answer is the principle 'He is not questioned about what He does.' This is not a refusal to answer but a theological affirmation that God's actions are not subject to human standards of necessity.
This principle is central to Ash'ari theology.
3
Reflect on its implication
Consider why this answer is sufficient. In kalam, it cuts off the chain of 'why' when it comes to divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن تعظيم أجورهم لا يتوقف على وقوع تلك الأمراض بهم؛ لجواز أن الله تعالى يُعَظِمُ أجورهم بدون ذلك. أجيب بأنه تعالى لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل.
For if it is said: Indeed, the magnification of their rewards does not depend on the occurrence of those illnesses upon them; because it is permissible for Allah Most High to magnify their rewards without that. The answer is that He Most High is not questioned about what He does.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objection claims that the reward for prophets and friends of Allah could be given without sending afflictions upon them, since Allah is able to reward directly. The answer is a powerful kalam principle: Allah is not questioned about what He does. This means His actions are not subject to human interrogation about cause or purpose; they are accepted as they are. The answer does not deny the possibility of alternative means, but asserts that the divine will is self-sufficient and beyond questioning.

Key Terms
تعظيم الأجور
الأمراض
لا يُسأل عما يفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer 'He is not questioned' means God's actions are random or without reason.
Correction: In kalam, it means God's actions are not subject to human standards of obligation; they are always wise but not compelled by external causes.

Checkpoint
What is the essence of the answer given to the objection that Allah could magnify rewards without afflicting prophets?
A) That Allah has promised to do so through afflictions only.
B) That afflictions are necessary for the reward to be real.
C) That Allah is not questioned about what He does.
D) That the objection is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are beyond human questioning; the sufficiency of this answer lies in affirming divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you ever find yourself questioning God's wisdom or fairness? How does the truth that 'He is not questioned about what He does' confront that tendency in you today?
?
Think of a recent disappointment or hardship. Did your inner reaction assume you had the right to know 'why'? What would it take for you to fully submit to the principle that Allah's actions are beyond interrogation?
?
If you truly believed—not just intellectually, but in your bones—that Allah is not questioned, what would change about how you pray, complain, or trust Him during difficulties? Make a concrete resolve starting now.

The Conditional Syllogism for Oneness and its Critique
Al-Bajuri presents and critiques the conditional syllogism for oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for God's oneness based on the conditional syllogism and its limitation noted by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
The first proof for oneness is sound but incomplete.

Al-Bajuri begins by explaining the author's initial argument for God's oneness, which uses a conditional syllogism: if there were a second god, everything would not depend on God; but everything does depend on Him, so a second god is impossible. However, al-Bajuri points out a deficiency in this proof: it only addresses one aspect of divinity. This lesson introduces that argument and its critique, setting the stage for a more comprehensive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the structure: first the statement of the proof, then the critique.
2
Understand the conditional syllogism
Identify the two premises: the conditional and the denial of the consequent. See how the conclusion eliminates a second god.
Al-Bajuri says this is a qiyās istithnā'ī.
3
Analyze the critique
Al-Bajuri finds the proof deficient because it only addresses one universal (kullī) among many. What does this mean? It means the proof does not cover all aspects of divinity.
The term 'bāqī al-kumūm' refers to the remaining universals.
4
Reflect on the purpose
This lesson shows that even a valid proof can be incomplete. It prepares for a stronger argument that covers all aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى أَيْضاً) أي: كما أوجب ما تقدم. (قوله: إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الْأُلُوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ... إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى قياس استثنائي، نظمه :هكذا: لو كان معه ثانٍ في الألوهية لما افتقر إليه شيء، لكنّ عَدَمَ افتقار شيء إليه ،باطل كيف وهو الذي يَفْتَقِرُ إليه كلُّ ما سواه، ولا يخفى ما في هذا الدليل من القصور؛ لعدم التعرُّض فيه لباقي الكموم. (وقوله: لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ أي: حين إذ كان معه ثانٍ في الألوهية. ووجه لزوم عجزهما حينئذ لأنه لا يخلو فإما أن يتفقا وإما أن يختلفا، وعلى كلّ يَلْزَمُ عجزهما، أما الأول؛ فلأنه يَلْزَمُ عليه اجتماعُ مُؤَثِّرَيْنِ على أَثَرٍ واحدٍ إِن أَوْجَدَاه معاً، وتحصيل الحاصل إن أوجداه مرتباً. وأما الثاني؛ فلأنه يلزم عليه اجتماع النقيضين إن نَفَذَ مرادُهما، وإن نَفَذَ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر؛ كان الذي لم يَنْفُذُ مراده عاجزاً، فيكون الآخرُ كذلك؛ لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما. (قوله: اجْتِمَاعُ مُؤَثِّرَيْنِ عَلَى أَثَرِ وَاحِدٍ أي على وجه استقلال كل منهما بتحصيل ذلك الأثر؛ إذ هذا هو المحكوم عليه بالاستحالة، بخلاف اجتماعهما عليه على سبيل المعاونة فإنه ممكن، لكن الألوهية تأبى ذلك، وتقتضي أن كل واحد منها يحصله على سبيل الاستقلال، وقد علمت أن تحصيله على هذا الوجه محال. (قوله: فَيَكُونُ الْآخَرُ كَذَلِكَ الحكم عليه بالعجز مع نفوذ مراده مما لا يعقل. (وقوله : لانْعِقَادِ الْمُمَاثَلَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا لا ينتج عجزه بعد نفوذ مراده، وإنما ينتج عدم نفوذ مراد واحد منهما ابتداء، وإذا لم ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين، ولا مراد أحدهما لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما؛ تعين عجزهما.
(And it necessitates for Him, exalted, also) i.e., as it necessitated what preceded. (His saying: 'for if there were with Him a second in divinity, nothing would depend upon Him... etc.') He indicated thereby a conditional syllogism, its arrangement is as follows: if there were with Him a second in divinity, nothing would depend upon Him; but the non-dependence of anything upon Him is false—how can it be when He is the one upon whom everything other than Him depends? And it is not hidden what deficiency there is in this proof, due to the lack of addressing the rest of the universals. (And His saying: 'for the necessity of their impotence then' i.e., when there is with Him a second in divinity. The reason for the necessity of their impotence then is that it is inevitable: either they agree or they differ; and in every case their impotence follows. As for the first: because it entails the conjunction of two efficient causes upon a single effect, if they produce it together, and the acquisition of the already acquired if they produce it in sequence. As for the second: because it entails the conjunction of two contradictories if both their wills are executed; and if the will of one is executed without the other, then the one whose will is not executed is impotent, so the other is likewise, due to the conclusion of similarity between them. (His saying: 'the conjunction of two efficient causes upon a single effect' means each independently producing that effect; for this is judged impossible, unlike their conjunction upon it by way of cooperation, for that is possible, but divinity precludes that and requires that each produce it independently, and you have already learned that producing it in this manner is impossible. (His saying: 'so the other is likewise' judging him to be impotent while his will is executed is unintelligible. (And His saying: 'due to the conclusion of similarity between them' does not yield his impotence after the execution of his will; rather, it yields the non-execution of the will of either from the start; and when their will is not executed due to the conjunction of contradictories that would follow, and the will of one of them is not executed due to the conclusion of similarity between them, the impotence of both is established.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the possibility of multiple gods (specifically two) and a proof of God's oneness. Al-Bajuri first explains that the author's argument is a conditional syllogism: if there were a second god, nothing would depend on God; but everything depends on Him, so a second god is impossible. However, al-Bajuri notes a weakness: this argument does not address other aspects of divinity (bāqī al-kumūm). Then he explains the stronger argument based on the impossibility of two omnipotent beings. If two gods existed, they would either both agree or differ. If they agree and attempt to create the same effect simultaneously, either they both create it independently (impossible, as this would require two independent agents for one effect) or in sequence (the second would be creating something already existing). If they differ, then either both wills are executed (leading to contradictory effects) or only one is executed (making the other impotent, and due to similarity, the first would also be impotent). Thus, in all cases, impotence follows, which is impossible for God. The commentary clarifies that the impossibility concerns independent action, not cooperation, but divinity demands independence.

Key Terms
ثانٍ في الألوهية
قياس استثنائي
باقي الكموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the mere fact that everything depends on God is sufficient to prove oneness.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that this proof is insufficient because it only addresses one aspect of divinity; a complete proof must address all universal attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Bajuri consider the first proof deficient?
A) Because it is logically invalid
B) Because it does not address the rest of the universals
C) Because it assumes cooperation between gods
D) Because it is based on a false premise

Key Takeaway
The first proof for oneness using the conditional syllogism is valid but incomplete; it must be supplemented by arguments addressing all divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than God depends on Him for its existence, then what does your daily life—your work, your relationships, your plans—actually depend on? Are you living as if everything depends on God, or as if it depends on you?
?
Al-Bajuri points out that the proof for oneness is incomplete because it only addresses one of God's universal attributes. How many of God's attributes have you truly contemplated—enough to shape your worship and reliance on Him? What are you neglecting?
?
This lesson shows that a valid argument can still be incomplete. In your own faith, are you content with a surface-level belief in oneness, or are you actively seeking a deeper, more comprehensive understanding of who God is—even if it requires effort and critique? What will you do today to move beyond a proof that is merely 'valid'?

The Impossibility of Two Omnipotent Beings
Al-Bajuri refutes the possibility of a second god by showing the impossibility of two omnipotent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stronger proof for God's oneness based on the impossibility of two omnipotent beings, which covers all possible scenarios.

Core Idea
Two gods would lead to contradiction or impotence, which is impossible.

Al-Bajuri now presents the decisive argument for oneness, analyzing every possible scenario when two omnipotent beings coexist. The argument shows that whether they agree or differ, their impotence necessarily follows. This proof is comprehensive because it addresses the core attribute of omnipotence. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the entire argument carefully. Notice it covers two main cases: agreement and disagreement.
2
Understand the case of agreement
If two gods both create the same effect, they either do so simultaneously (leading to two independent causes for one effect, impossible) or sequentially (the second creates what already exists, also impossible).
Al-Bajuri clarifies that this impossibility is only when each acts independently; cooperation is possible but not for gods.
3
Understand the case of disagreement
If they differ, either both wills are executed (leading to contradictory effects) or only one is executed (making the other impotent, and due to similarity, both are impotent).
The similarity between them entails that if one is impotent, the other is also.
4
Reflect on the strength of this proof
This argument covers all possibilities and addresses the attribute of power directly, making it more comprehensive than the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله: إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ ثَانٍ فِي الْأُلُوهِيَّةِ لَمَا افْتَقَرَ... إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى قياس استثنائي، نظمه :هكذا: لو كان معه ثانٍ في الألوهية لما افتقر إليه شيء، لكنّ عَدَمَ افتقار شيء إليه ،باطل كيف وهو الذي يَفْتَقِرُ إليه كلُّ ما سواه، ولا يخفى ما في هذا الدليل من القصور؛ لعدم التعرُّض فيه لباقي الكموم. (وقوله: لِلْزُومِ عَجْزِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ أي: حين إذ كان معه ثانٍ في الألوهية. ووجه لزوم عجزهما حينئذ لأنه لا يخلو فإما أن يتفقا وإما أن يختلفا، وعلى كلّ يَلْزَمُ عجزهما، أما الأول؛ فلأنه يَلْزَمُ عليه اجتماعُ مُؤَثِّرَيْنِ على أَثَرٍ واحدٍ إِن أَوْجَدَاه معاً، وتحصيل الحاصل إن أوجداه مرتباً. وأما الثاني؛ فلأنه يلزم عليه اجتماع النقيضين إن نَفَذَ مرادُهما، وإن نَفَذَ مراد أحدهما دون الآخر؛ كان الذي لم يَنْفُذُ مراده عاجزاً، فيكون الآخرُ كذلك؛ لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما. (وقوله : لانْعِقَادِ الْمُمَاثَلَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا لا ينتج عجزه بعد نفوذ مراده، وإنما ينتج عدم نفوذ مراد واحد منهما ابتداء، وإذا لم ينفذ مرادهما لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين، ولا مراد أحدهما لانعقاد المماثلة بينهما؛ تعين عجزهما.

However, al-Bajuri notes a weakness: this argument does not address other aspects of divinity (bāqī al-kumūm). Then he explains the stronger argument based on the impossibility of two omnipotent beings. If two gods existed, they would either both agree or differ. If they agree and attempt to create the same effect simultaneously, either they both create it independently (impossible, as this would require two independent agents for one effect) or in sequence (the second would be creating something already existing). If they differ, then either both wills are executed (leading to contradictory effects) or only one is executed (making the other impotent, and due to similarity, the first would also be impotent). Thus, in all cases, impotence follows, which is impossible for God. The commentary clarifies that the impossibility concerns independent action, not cooperation, but divinity demands independence.

Key Terms
اجتماع مؤثرين
تحصيل الحاصل
اجتماع النقيضين
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two gods cooperate, they could avoid contradiction.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that cooperation is possible in principle, but divinity requires each god to be fully independent; thus cooperation is not admissible for gods.

Checkpoint
What happens if two gods disagree and only one's will is executed?
A) They still work together
B) The one whose will is not executed is impotent, and the other becomes impotent due to similarity
C) A contradiction occurs
D) Nothing happens

Key Takeaway
The argument from the impossibility of two omnipotent beings is a decisive proof for God's oneness, as every possible scenario leads to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that only one God is worthy of worship and that any partnership would lead to absurdity, why do you still sometimes fear created beings more than Allah? What is one specific relationship you can reorder this week by consciously fearing Allah's displeasure above all else?
?
When you feel torn between your desires and Allah's commands, are you acting as if there is a second authority that can override His will? What aspect of your life reveals a hidden 'partner'—such as relying on your own strength or seeking human approval? Commit today to a concrete practice that affirms His sole sovereignty in that area.
?
The proof shows that any partner would necessitate weakness or contradiction. In your daily worship, do you ever sense a drift toward showing off (riya) or seeking praise? How can you purify your next act of worship so it is solely for Allah, even if no one sees?

The Second Proof of Tawhid: Conditional Syllogism Against a Second God
Al-Bajuri expounds the second proof against a second god using a conditional syllogism and analyzes the logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri formulates the second proof of divine unity using a conditional syllogism and why two independent agents are impossible.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because they would either contradict each other or produce an impossible effect together.

This lesson unpacks the logical backbone of the second proof against polytheism. Al-Bajuri presents a conditional syllogism: if there were a second god, nothing would need God, but everything does need Him, so a second is impossible. He then analyses the scenarios of agreement and disagreement to show that both lead to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional syllogism
Identify the premises: 'If there were a second god, nothing would need Him; but everything needs Him, so no second god.' Notice the commentator's remark about its deficiency.
The syllogism is of the exceptional type (قياس استثنائي).
2
Understand the impossibility of two agents
Focus on the two possibilities: agreement or disagreement. See why agreement leads to either simultaneous causation (impossible) or sequential causation (acquisition of the already acquired).
Disagreement leads to either both wills failing or one succeeding and the other being incapable.
3
Grasp the concept of independence
The marginal gloss clarifies that the impossibility is when each acts independently, not cooperatively. Divinity requires independence.
Cooperation would imply dependence, contrary to divinity.

As for the first, because it necessitates the conjunction of two agents upon one effect if they produce it together, and the acquisition of what is already acquired if they produce it in sequence. As for the second, because it necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories if both their wills are executed, and if the will of one is executed and not the other, the one whose will is not executed is incapable, and the other is likewise, due to the analogy between them. (His saying: 'conjunction of two agents upon one effect' i.e., in the manner of each independently producing that effect; for this is what is judged impossible, unlike their conjunction upon it by way of assistance, for that is possible, but divinity precludes that and necessitates that each one produces it independently, and you have known that producing it in this manner is impossible. (His saying: 'then the other is likewise' judging him with incapability despite the execution of his will is unintelligible. (His saying: 'due to the analogy between them' does not produce his incapability after the execution of his will, but rather produces the non-execution of the will of either initially, and when neither their will is executed due to what necessitates the conjunction of contradictories, nor the will of one due to the analogy between them, their incapability is determined.
